Zakat on Debts, Mal al-Dimar, and Payment Methods
Miscellaneous zakat rulings with intra-Hanafi proofs and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on mal al-dimar and apply the principles of weight versus value when paying zakat on gold and silver.

Core Idea
Mal al-dimar exempts past zakat; weight rules when paying same metal; value rules when adding different metals.

This lesson covers key miscellaneous rulings on zakat: when debts are received, whether past years' zakat is due on hard-to-recover property (mal al-dimar), and the important distinction between paying zakat from the same metal versus a different kind. We will explore the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether weight or value governs payment, and how gold and silver are combined to reach the nisab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five zakat issues in the passage
First, locate the rulings on: (1) zakat on received debts, (2) mal al-dimar exemption, (3) discharge of debt as zakat, (4) payment from goods or same/different metals, and (5) adding gold and silver for nisab.
The passage flows from debts to mal al-dimar to payment methods.
2
State the ruling on mal al-dimar
Mal al-dimar is property whose recovery is impossible or extremely difficult. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), when such property is later recovered, no zakat is due for past years, regardless of amount. Examples: runaway slave, lost property without evidence, property buried in desert and forgotten, etc.
If evidence exists or recovery is possible (e.g., buried in a safe place), zakat is obligatory.
3
Apply the principle of weight in payment from the same metal
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that when paying zakat from the same type of gold or silver, the weight of the payment must match the required weight. Value is not considered. For example, paying five inferior dirhams for an obligation of five good dirhams is valid but disliked (karaha).
Zafar holds that value is considered; Muhammad holds that the more beneficial for the poor is considered.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on payment
The ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf (weight), Muhammad (beneficial), and Zafar (value) affects cases like paying good for inferior or crafted silver jugs. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (weight).
When paying from a different metal (e.g., gold for silver), value is considered by consensus.
5
Analyze the ruling on adding gold and silver for nisab
When one possesses both gold and silver, Abu Hanifa adds their values to determine if the combined sum reaches the nisab. The Sahibayn add by parts (weight). For example, 100 dirhams + 5 dinars worth 100 dirhams: zakat due according to Abu Hanifa, not according to the Sahibayn.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is more common.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل شتی
وأوجبا عن المقبوض من الديون الثلاثة بحسابه مطلقاً، وإذا قبض مال الضمار لا تحب الصاحبان قليلاً أو كثيراً زكاة السنين الماضية، وهو كآبق ومفقود ومغصوب ليس عليه بينة، ومال ساقط في إذا كانا من عبيد التجارة البحر، ومدفون في مفازة أو دار عظيمة وقد نسي مكانه ومأخوذ مصادرة، ومودع عند من لا يعرفه ودين لا بينة عليه ولا يجزئ عن الزكاة دين أبرئ عنه فقير بنيتها، الجملة نعت لدين وصح دفع عرض ومكيل وموزون عن زكاة النقدين بالقيمة، وإن أدى من عين النقدين، فالمعتبر وزنهما أداء كما اعتبر ،وجوباً، وتضم قيمة العروض إلى الثمنين..... مال الضمار : هو مال تعذر الوصول إليه مع قيام الملك. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٧١٦] ليس عليه: فلو كان له بينة تجب لما مضى. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٧١٦] في مفازة أما المدفون في حرز ، سواء كان داره أم دار غيره، فتجب؛ لإمكان التوصل إليه بالحفر. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٧١٦] مصادرة بأن يأمره الظالم بإتيان ماله ثم يردّه. من لا يعرفه أما إن كانت عند معارفه وجبت الزكاة لتفريطه بالنسيان في غير محله [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٧١٦] ولا يجزئ إلخ : أي لو كان لمالك النصاب دين على أحد فأبرأه عنه ناوياً أداء زكاته، لا يجزئ عنها. فالمعتبر إلخ: أي يعتبر في الذهب والفضة أن يكون المؤدى قدر الواجب وزناً، ولا تعتبر فيه القيمة، وكذا في حق أن يبلغ وزنهما نصاباً، الوجوب يعتبر ولا تعتبر فيه القيمة، أما الأول وهو اعتبار الوزن في الأداء، فهو قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف حمله، وقال زفر له تعتبر القيمة، وقال محمد له يعتبر الأنفع للفقراء، حتى لو أدى عن خمسة دراهم جياد خمسة زيوفاً قيمتها أربعة دراهم جياد جاز عندهما ويكره، وقال محمد وزفر: لا يجوز حتى يؤدي الفضل؛ لأن زفر يعتبر القيمة، ومحمد يعتبر الأنفع وهما يعتبران الوزن، ولو أدى أربعة جيدة قيمتها خمسة رديئة عن خمسة رديئة لا يجوز إلا عند زفر ؛ لما بينا ولو كان له إبريق فضة وزنه مائتان، وقيمته لصناعته ثلاث مائة إن أدى العين يؤدي ربع عشره وهو خمسة، جاز عندهما، وقال محمد وزفر: لا يجوز إلا أن يؤدي الفضل، ولو أدّى . من خلاف جنسه تعتبر القيمة بالإجماع [تبيين الحقائق: ٧٤/٢] وتضم قيمة إلخ: أي تضم قيمة العروض إلى الذهب والفضة، ويضم الذهب إلى الفضة بالقيمة، فيكمل به النصاب، وما ذكره الشيخ محله من أ أن أحدهما يضم إلى الآخر بالقيمة قول أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يضم بالأجزاء، حتى لو كان له مائة درهم وخمسة دنانير قيمتها مائة درهم تجب فيها الزكاة عنده خلافا لهما، وعكسه لو كان له مائة ، درهم وعشرة دنانير قيمتها لا تبلع مائة درهم ،تجب عندهما ولا تجب عنده كذا ذكره بعضهم، وفيه نظر؛ لأنه إذا كانت عشرة دنانير لا تبلغ مائة . درهم فالمائة تبلغ عشرة دنانير ضرورة تبيين الحقائق بحذف: ۸۰/۲، ۸۲]
من
Miscellaneous issues: The two companions [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] made obligatory zakat on what is received from the three types of debts by their calculation absolutely. When one receives mal al-dimar (property whose recovery is difficult), zakat for past years is not obligatory, whether little or much, according to both. Examples: a runaway slave, lost property, a usurped article with no evidence, property fallen into the sea, buried in a desert or a large house whose location is forgotten, taken by confiscation, deposited with someone unknown, and a debt with no evidence. It is not valid for zakat to discharge a poor person of a debt with the intention of zakat (the phrase is an adjective for 'debt'). It is valid to pay from goods, weighed or measured items, for zakat on gold and silver by value. If one pays from the same kind (gold or silver), the weight is considered in payment just as it is considered for obligation. The value of goods is added to the two metals. [Margin of al-Tahtawi: 716] Mal al-dimar: property whose access is impossible despite ownership. [Margin] 'No evidence': if he had evidence, zakat would be obligatory for the past. [Margin] Buried in a desert: as for buried in a safe place, whether his house or another's, zakat is obligatory because recovery is possible by digging. [Margin] Confiscation: the oppressor orders him to bring his wealth then returns it. [Margin] Deposited with one he does not know: if it is with acquaintances, zakat is obligatory due to negligence in forgetting. [Margin] 'It is not valid etc.': meaning if the owner of nisab has a debt on someone and discharges him intending his zakat payment, it does not suffice. [Margin] 'So the weight is considered etc.': meaning that in gold and silver, what is paid must be the obligatory amount by weight, and value is not considered; similarly regarding reaching nisab, obligation is considered by weight, not value. As for the first (weight in payment), it is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Zafar said value is considered; Muhammad said the more beneficial for the poor is considered. So if he pays for five good dirhams, five inferior dirhams whose value is four good dirhams, it is permissible according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) but disliked; Muhammad and Zafar say not permissible until he pays the difference. If he pays four good dirhams whose value is five inferior dirhams for five inferior dirhams, it is not permissible except according to Zafar. If he has a silver jug weighing two hundred, and its value due to craftsmanship is three hundred, if he pays the same kind, he pays one-tenth of its weight, which is five; it is permissible according to the two; Muhammad and Zafar say not permissible unless he pays the extra. If he pays from a different kind, value is considered by consensus. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq] The value of goods is added: meaning the value of goods is added to gold and silver, and gold is added to silver by value, to complete the nisab. What the author mentioned is that one is added to the other by value according to Abu Hanifa; according to the two, they are added by parts. So if he has one hundred dirhams and five dinars whose value is one hundred dirhams, zakat is obligatory according to him, contrary to them. The opposite: if he has one hundred dirhams and ten dinars whose value does not reach one hundred dirhams, it is obligatory according to them and not according to him. Some mentioned this, but there is consideration; because if ten dinars do not reach one hundred dirhams, then one hundred dirhams necessarily reach ten dinars. (From Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, abridged)
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage contains miscellaneous rulings on zakat. It begins by stating that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that zakat is obligatory on all three types of debts (middle, weak, and what?) upon receipt, proportionally. Then it discusses 'mal al-dimar'—property that cannot be recovered easily—for which no zakat is due on past years. Examples include a runaway slave, lost property without proof, etc. The key condition is that if evidence exists or recovery is possible (e.g., buried in a safe place), zakat is due. Next, it rules that forgiving a debt with the intention of zakat does not count as payment. It discusses payment of zakat: paying from goods or weighed/measured items is valid by value for gold/silver zakat. For payment from the same metal, weight is considered, not value, based on Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Zafar says value is considered; Muhammad says the more beneficial for the poor. Examples illustrate: paying inferior silver for good silver is permissible but disliked; the dispute over paying good for inferior. For a silver jug with artistic value, the weight determines zakat, not the market value. If paying from a different metal (e.g., gold for silver), value is considered by consensus. Finally, it addresses adding values: when one has mix of gold and silver, Abu Hanifa adds by value, while the two add by weight (parts). This affects whether nisab is reached and zakat is due.

Key Terms
مال الضمار (mal al-dimar): Property whose recovery is impossible or extremely difficult despite ownership.
عرض (ard): Goods or commodities other than gold, silver, and currency.
الاعتبار بالوزن (al-i'tibar bi al-wazn): The principle that zakat liability and payment for gold and silver is based on weight, not value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, when paying zakat on silver from the same silver, what is considered?
A) The weight of the silver paid.
B) The value of the silver paid.
C) The most beneficial for the poor.
D) The value of the silver compared to gold.

Key Takeaway
Mal al-dimar exempts past zakat; weight governs payment from same metal; value governs adding different metals and payment from goods.

Zakat on Collected Debts and Mal al-Dimar
Ruling on zakat for collected debts and mal al-dimar with condition of evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on zakat for collected debts and for mal al-dimar when recovered, and identify the condition of evidence that changes the obligation.

Core Idea
Zakat on mal al-dimar is not due for past years unless evidence existed — access matters more than ownership.

This passage from Nur al-Idah addresses two important zakat scenarios: debts collected after a period and property that was inaccessible (mal al-dimar). The ruling distinguishes between wealth that was always accessible and wealth that was practically lost. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying zakat on recovered assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two themes
The passage covers two separate rulings: one about debts collected after a delay, and another about mal al-dimar (property that is inaccessible despite ownership).
Keep these distinct as they have different conditions.
2
Define mal al-dimar
Recognize the examples given: runaway slave, missing person, usurped property without evidence, lost in sea, buried and forgotten, confiscated, deposit with unknown person, debt without evidence. The common condition is lack of access or evidence.
The term 'mal al-dimar' itself means property whose access is difficult.
3
Note the ruling for each category
For collected debts: zakat is wajib in proportion to the amount collected. For mal al-dimar without evidence: no zakat is due for past years according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
The ruling for mal al-dimar is explicitly attributed to the Sahibayn.
4
Apply the exception
If the owner had evidence (e.g., a document) that could have proven the property, then zakat for past years is wajib. Also, if the property was buried in a secure place, zakat is wajib because access was possible.
Evidence and accessibility are the key factors that change the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأوجبا عن المقبوض من الديون الثلاثة بحسابه مطلقاً، وإذا قبض مال الضمار لا تحب الصاحبان[؟] قليلاً أو كثيراً زكاة السنين الماضية، وهو كآبق ومفقود ومغصوب ليس عليه بينة، ومال ساقط في البحر، ومدفون في مفازة أو دار عظيمة وقد نسي مكانه ومأخوذ مصادرة، ومودع عند من لا يعرفه ودين لا بينة عليه . مال الضمار : هو مال تعذر الوصول إليه مع قيام الملك. ليس عليه: فلو كان له بينة تجب لما مضى. في مفازة أما المدفون في حرز ، سواء كان داره أم دار غيره، فتجب؛ لإمكان التوصل إليه بالحفر. مصادرة بأن يأمره الظالم بإتيان ماله ثم يردّه. من لا يعرفه أما إن كانت عند معارفه وجبت الزكاة لتفريطه بالنسيان في غير محله
Zakat is obligatory on collected debts from the three types in proportion absolutely. And when one takes possession of mal al-dimar (property difficult to access), zakat for past years is not obligatory according to the two companions, whether little or much. And it is like a runaway slave, a missing person, a usurped property without evidence, property fallen into the sea, property buried in a desert or a large house whose location is forgotten, property taken by confiscation, a deposit with someone unknown, and a debt without evidence. Mal al-dimar is property whose access is difficult despite ownership. 'No evidence': if he had evidence, zakat would be obligatory for what has passed. 'In a desert': as for property buried in a secure place, whether his house or another's, zakat is obligatory due to the possibility of access by digging. 'Confiscation': by the tyrant ordering him to bring his property then returning it. 'Unknown person': as for if it is with acquaintances, zakat is obligatory due to his negligence in forgetting in an inappropriate place.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents two related rulings. First, concerning debts: when a debt is collected, zakat must be paid for the years it was due, in proportion to the amount collected. Second, concerning mal al-dimar—property that is inaccessible despite ownership—if it is later recovered, zakat for previous years is not obligatory according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The commentary clarifies the definition: mal al-dimar includes runaway slaves, missing persons, usurped property without evidence, items lost at sea, buried property whose location is forgotten, confiscated property, deposits with unknown persons, and debts without evidence. The key condition is the lack of evidence or ability to access. If the owner had evidence, zakat becomes obligatory. Similarly, if the property was in a secure place like his own home, he is considered negligent for forgetting, and zakat is due. The ruling emphasizes that obligation depends on practical ability to recover the wealth, not mere ownership.

Key Terms
مال الضمار (māl al-ḍimār): Property whose access is difficult despite the owner's ownership, such as lost, buried, or confiscated items without evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When there is no evidence according to the two companions
C) When the owner is negligent
D) When the property is a debt

Key Takeaway
Zakat on mal al-dimar for past years is not required unless the owner had evidence enabling access.

Nisab and the Effect of Mid-Year Deficiency
States nisab values and the rule that deficiency in between does not harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that nisab must be present at the start and end of the year, and apply it to distinguish between complete loss of wealth (which breaks the year) and partial transformation (which does not).

Core Idea
Nisab must be present at start and end; a temporary dip does not break the year unless wealth is completely destroyed.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi principle regarding nisab continuity in zakat. While many assume nisab must be maintained throughout the entire lunar year, the Hanafi school holds that only the beginning and end matter. The passage provides two contrasting examples to clarify when a mid-year change resets the zakat year and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab thresholds
Note the values: 20 mithqals for gold, 200 dirhams of silver (with the specific weight ratio). These are the minimum amounts for zakat liability.
Gold nisab is about 85 grams; silver nisab is about 595 grams in modern measures.
2
Understand the principle of deficiency
The rule: if nisab is complete at the beginning and end of the year, a temporary drop in between does not nullify zakat. Zakat is still due at year-end.
This differs from the Shafi'i school which requires nisab throughout the year.
3
Analyze the first example (grape juice to vinegar)
A person buys juice worth 200 dirhams. It becomes wine (khamr), which is non-wealth in Islam, then becomes vinegar worth 200 dirhams. The year is broken because the original asset was completely destroyed (wine is not a valuable asset). A new year starts from the time the vinegar appears.
The key is total loss of the original wealth.
4
Analyze the second example (sheep to hides)
Sheep worth 200 dirhams all die, but their hides are tanned and become worth 200 dirhams. The year continues because wool, hair, and horns retain some value even after death; the wealth was not entirely obliterated.
Partial retention of value keeps the year intact.
5
Derive the distinction
The deciding factor is whether any part of the original wealth remains as a valuable asset. If yes, the year continues; if no, the year resets.
This is a nuanced application of the principle that 'deficiency does not harm' but complete extinction does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصاب الذهب عشرون مثقالاً، ونصاب الفضة مائتا درهم من الدراهم التي كل عشرة منها وزن سبعة مثاقيل، وما زاد على نصاب، ونقصان النصاب: أي إذا كان النصاب كاملاً في ابتداء الحول وانتهائه، فنقصانه فيما بين ذلك لا يسقط الزكاة، وعلى هذا قالوا: إذا اشترى عصيراً للتجارة يساوي مائتي درهم في أثناء الحول، ثم تخلل، والخل يساوي مائتي درهم يستأنف الحول للخل، وبطل الحول الأول، ولو اشترى شياهًا تساوي مائتي درهم، فماتت كلها ودبغ جلدها وصار يساوي مائتي درهم لا يبطل الحول الأول، بل يزكيها إذا تم الحول الأول من وقت الشراء، والفرق بينهما: أن الخمر إذا تخمرت هلكت كلها وصارت غير مال، فانقطع الحول، ثم بالتخلل صار مالاً غير الأول، والشياه إذا ماتت لم يهلك كل المال؛ لأن شعرها وصوفها وقرنها لم يخرج من أن يكون مالاً، فلم يبطل الحول لبقاء البعض.
The nisab of gold is twenty mithqals, and the nisab of silver is two hundred dirhams from the dirhams of which every ten have the weight of seven mithqals. And what exceeds the nisab? Deficiency of nisab: meaning if the nisab is complete at the beginning of the year and its end, then its deficiency in between does not nullify zakat. Upon this they said: If one buys juice for trade worth two hundred dirhams in the middle of the year, then it becomes vinegar, and the vinegar is worth two hundred dirhams, the year is started anew for the vinegar, and the first year is nullified. And if one buys sheep worth two hundred dirhams, and they all die and their skins are tanned and become worth two hundred dirhams, the first year is not nullified; rather he gives zakat on them when the first year is completed from the time of purchase. The difference between them: that wine (juice) when it becomes impure, becomes entirely destroyed and becomes non-wealth, so the year is cut off, then by becoming vinegar it becomes wealth other than the first. And the sheep when they die, not all wealth is destroyed because their hair, wool, and horns have not ceased to be wealth, so the year is not nullified due to the remaining part.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with comparative examples. It first establishes the nisab thresholds: 20 mithqals for gold and 200 dirhams for silver, with the specific weight standard where 10 dirhams equal 7 mithqals. The key ruling: a deficiency in the nisab during the course of the year does not invalidate zakat, provided the nisab is complete at both the beginning and the end of the lunar year. The author then provides two contrasting examples to illustrate when a complete loss of the original asset breaks the year. First, if someone buys grape juice for trade worth 200 dirhams (meeting the silver nisab), and during the year it ferments into wine (khamr) and then becomes vinegar (khal), the original asset (juice) is considered entirely destroyed when it becomes wine (since wine is non-wealth), so the year for that asset ends. When it turns into vinegar, a new asset is created, and a new year begins from that point. Second, if someone buys sheep for trade worth 200 dirhams, and all the sheep die, but their hides are tanned and become worth 200 dirhams, the year is not broken. This is because the wool, hair, and horns remain wealth even after death, so some wealth persists, and the original year continues. The distinction centers on whether the original wealth was obliterated or merely transformed while retaining some value.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth subject to zakat.
الحول (al-hawl): The lunar year that must pass for zakat to be due.
نقصان النصاب (nuqsan al-nisab): Temporary drop in the value of zakatable wealth below nisab during the year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The year is broken; a new year starts from the time the wealth returns to nisab.
B) Zakat is still due at the end of the original year.
C) No zakat is due because the nisab was not maintained.
D) Zakat is due only on the amount that remained above nisab throughout.

Key Takeaway
Nisab must be present at the beginning and end of the lunar year; temporary mid-year deficiency does not void zakat, unless the original wealth is completely destroyed.

The Standard Dirham Weight: Definition and Historical Derivation
Defines the weight of dirham and mithqal with historical context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the standard weight of the legal dirham (14 qirats) and explain its derivation from the three historical dirham types.

Core Idea
The legal dirham is 14 qirats — a composite standard fixed to end transactional disputes.

Zakat on silver is based on a specific weight of dirhams. But what exactly is a 'dirham' in Islamic law? This passage clarifies the standard weight of the dirham (14 qirats) and traces its historical origin. Understanding this standard is essential for correctly calculating the nisab for zakat. Let's explore how the weight was fixed and why it matters for fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key weight units
Extract the definitions of mithqal, dirham, and qirat. Note their relationships: 1 mithqal = 20 qirats; 1 dirham = 14 qirats; 1 qirat = 5 barley grains.
These are absolute standard weights, not approximations.
2
Understand the historical problem
Three types of dirhams existed (20, 12, and 10 qirats). This caused disputes in payments. Note the ratios relative to the dinar (which is the mithqal).
The first type matched the dinar, the second was 3/5, the third was 1/2.
3
Trace the standardization process
A composite dirham was created by taking one of each type, mixing them, and dividing into three equal parts. Each resulting dirham weighed 14 qirats.
14 qirats = (20+12+10)/3 = 42/3 = 14.
4
Apply the standard to zakat
The nisab of silver is 200 dirhams of this standard weight. This ensures uniformity in zakat calculations.
If dirhams are lighter or heavier, adjustments are needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل عشرة إلخ أي يعتبر أن يكون وزن كل عشرة دراهم وزن سبعة مثاقيل، والمثقال: - وهو الدينار عشرون قيراطاً، والدرهم أربعة عشر قيراطاً، والقيراط خمس شعيرات، والأصل فيه: أن الدراهم كانت مختلفة في زمن النبي وفي زمن أبي بكر وعمر ما على ثلاث مراتب: فبعضها كان عشرين قيراطاً مثل الدينار، وبعضها كان اثني عشر قيراطاً ثلاثة أخماس الدينار وبعضها عشرة قراريط نصف الدينار، فالأول: وزن عشرة أي العشرة منه وزن العشرة من الدنانير والثاني: وزن ستة أي كل عشرة منه وزن ستة من الدنانير، والثالث: وزن خمسة، أي كل عشرة منه وزن خمسة دنانير، فوقع التنازع بين الناس في الإيفاء والاستيفاء، فأخذ من كل نوع درهماً، فخلطه فجعله ثلاثة دراهم متساوية، فخرج كل درهم أربعة عشر قيراطاً، فبقي العمل عليه إلى يومنا هذا في كل شيء.
Every ten etc. meaning it is considered that the weight of every ten dirhams is the weight of seven mithqals. The mithqal—which is the dinar—is twenty qirats, and the dirham is fourteen qirats, and the qirat is five barley grains. The origin of this: that dirhams were of three types in the time of the Prophet and in the time of Abu Bakr and Umar. Some were twenty qirats like the dinar, some were twelve qirats (three-fifths of a dinar), and some were ten qirats (half a dinar). The first: weight of ten, i.e., ten of them equal the weight of ten dinars. The second: weight of six, i.e., ten of them equal the weight of six dinars. The third: weight of five, i.e., ten of them equal the weight of five dinars. A dispute arose among people regarding payment and receipt. So he took one dirham from each type, mixed them, and made three equal dirhams. Thus each dirham came out as fourteen qirats. And the practice has remained upon this until our day in everything.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition and historical background of the dirham weight standard used in zakat calculations. The key point: the nisab of silver is 200 dirhams, each of which must be 14 qirats. The author explains that the legal dirham is derived from the mithqal (dinar, a gold coin) which is 20 qirats. A qirat is defined as five barley grains. Historically, there were three types of dirhams in circulation: the full-weight (20 qirats, equal to a dinar), the medium (12 qirats, three-fifths of a dinar), and the light (10 qirats, half a dinar). This caused confusion in transactions. To standardize, a composite dirham was created by taking one of each type, mixing them, and making three equal dirhams. The resulting dirham weighed 14 qirats. This standard has been maintained ever since. The significance for zakat: the nisab of silver is 200 dirhams of this standard weight.

Key Terms
مثقال (mithqal): A unit of weight equal to 20 qirats; also a gold coin (dinar).
قيراط (qirat): A unit of weight equal to 5 barley grains; 1/20 of a mithqal.
درهم (dirham): A silver coin/weight of 14 qirats, the standard for zakat nisab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) 14 qirats

D) 10 qirats

Key Takeaway
The legal dirham weighs exactly 14 qirats (70 barley grains), a standard fixed from the average of three historical dirham types.

Zakat on Excess Over Nisab: The Five-Nisab Increment Rule
Ruling on zakat for amounts exceeding nisab with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi five-nisab increment rule for zakat on excess, apply it to numerical examples, and contrast it with the Sahibayn's proportional view.

Core Idea
Excess over nisab is exempt until it accumulates to five complete nisabs—fractions do not incur zakat.

This passage presents a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute regarding zakat on wealth that exceeds the nisab. Imam Abu Hanifa ruled that any amount above the nisab (200 dirhams for silver) is exempt (afw) until it reaches a multiple of five nisabs (i.e., an additional 800 dirhams). His two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn), disagreed, holding that any excess should be subject to zakat proportionally. The practical difference is illustrated with concrete numerical examples.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on excess
Imam Abu Hanifa: excess over the nisab is afw (exempt) until it amounts to five complete nisabs. Only when the total reaches a multiple of five nisabs does zakat become due on that increment.
This means fractions between 200 and 1000 dirhams are not zakatable.
2
State the Sahibayn's opposing view
Sahibayn hold that any excess, however small, is subject to zakat proportionally. There is no exemption for fractions.
Their view is closer to the position of other madhhabs.
3
Analyze the 205-dirham example
For 205 dirhams over two years: Imam says total zakat = 10 dirhams (5 per year, ignoring the 5-dirham excess). Sahibayn say total = 5 dirhams (since the 5-dirham excess in year one reduces the nisab for year two).
Imam's reasoning: 'la zakat fil kusur' (no zakat on fractions).
4
Analyze the 1000-dirham example
For 1000 dirhams over three years: Imam says zakat due: year 1 = 25, year 2 = 24, year 3 = 23. Sahibayn say year 1 = 25, year 2 = 24 + 3/8, year 3 = 23 + 1/2 + 1/4 + 1/8. The difference arises from treating fractions.
Imam's decreasing amounts reflect that after each payment, the wealth drops below a multiple of five nisabs, so the next year's zakat is calculated on the lower base.
5
Identify the consensus and mufta bihi
There is no dispute on the first year's zakat: both agree on 25 dirhams for 1000 dirhams. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Imam Abu Hanifa's view.
Al-Hidayah and later works prefer Imam's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زاد على نصاب، وما زاد إلخ أي ما زاد على النصاب عفو إلى أن يبلغ خمس نصاب، ثم كل ما زاد على الخمس عفو إلى أن يبلغ خمساً آخر، وقالا: ما زاد بحسابه ويظهر أثر الخلاف فيما لو كان مائتان وخمسة دراهم مضى عليها عامان، قال الإمام: يلزمه عشرة، وقالا خمسة؛ لأنه وجب عليه في العام الأول خمسة وثمن درهم فبقي السالم من الدين في الثاني نصاب إلا ثمنا، وعنده: لا زكاة في الكسور، فبقي النصاب في الثاني كاملاً، وفيما إذا كان له ألف وحال عليها ثلاثة أحوال كان عليه في الثاني أربعة وعشرون، وفي الثالث ثلاثة وعشرون عنده وقالا يجب مع الأربعة والعشرين ثلاثة أثمان درهم، ومع الثلاثة والعشرين نصف وربع وثمن درهم، ولا خلاف أنه يجب في الأول خمسة وعشرون. وبلغ خمسا زكاه بحسابه.
And what exceeds the nisab? And what exceeds etc. meaning what exceeds the nisab is exempt until it reaches five nisabs. Then each excess beyond five is exempt until it reaches another five. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: what exceeds is in proportion. The effect of the difference appears if there were two hundred and five dirhams and two years passed. Imam said: he owes ten. They said: five. Because in the first year he owed five and one-eighth dirham, so the amount remaining from the debt in the second year is nisab except one-eighth. In his view, there is no zakat on fractions, so the nisab remained complete in the second year. And if there were one thousand dirhams and three years passed, according to him in the second year he owes twenty-four, in the third twenty-three. And they said: along with the twenty-four, he owes three-eighths of a dirham, and with the twenty-three, half plus quarter plus eighth of a dirham. And there is no dispute that in the first year he owes twenty-five. And when it reaches five, he gives zakat in proportion.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding zakat on amounts that exceed the nisab. The position of Imam Abu Hanifa is that any excess over the nisab is exempt (afw) until it becomes a complete five nisabs (e.g., 200 dirhams → 400 dirhams nisab, then 400–600 is exempt, etc.). Only when the total reaches a multiple of five nisabs does zakat become due on that increment. In contrast, the two disciples (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that any excess is subject to zakat proportionally. The practical difference is illustrated with examples: if someone has 205 dirhams (5 dirhams above nisab) and two years pass, Imam says he owes 10 dirhams total (5 dirhams per year for 200 dirhams nisab, but since the 5 dirham excess is exempt, the second year's nisab remains intact). Sahibayn say he owes 5 dirhams total because the leftover from first year (after paying zakat on the excess fraction) reduces the nisab for the second year. Another example: 1000 dirhams over three years. Imam calculates 25 for first year (zakat on five nisabs: 5 × 5 = 25?), actually he says 25 first year, then 24 second, 23 third. Sahibayn include fractions. The text ends with 'When it reaches five, he gives zakat in proportion'—this likely supports Imam's view that only five nisab increments trigger zakat.

Key Terms
عفو (afw): An amount exempt from zakat; not subject to zakat until it accumulates to a multiple of the nisab.
خمس نصاب (khams nisab): Five times the nisab; a threshold for zakat on excess.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 5 dirhams
B) 5.125 dirhams
C) 10 dirhams
D) 0 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi five-nisab increment rule exempts fractions below five nisabs from zakat, unlike the Sahibayn's proportional approach.

Zakat on Jewelry, Mixed Metals, and Trade Goods Valuation
Ruling on zakat for jewelry, adulterated coins, and trade goods with price fluctuation dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on zakat for personal jewelry, adulterated coins, and trade goods with price fluctuation, and distinguish between Imam's and Sahibayn's positions on valuation date.

Core Idea
Jewelry is exempt unless for trade; trade goods value is assessed at year-end per the relied-upon view.

This passage from Nur al-Idah delivers three distinct zakat rulings: on jewelry, on mixed-metal coins, and on trade goods whose price fluctuates. The latter includes a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on when to assess the value for zakat. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correct zakat calculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for jewels and pearls
The text states there is no zakat on jewels and pearls unless they are owned with the intention of trade, like other trade goods. This means personal jewelry is exempt.
The exemption applies to personal use, not to jewelry held for resale.
2
Understand the treatment of adulterated coins
The text says 'what is predominated by adulteration is like the pure of the two metals.' That is, if a coin is mostly base metal, it is not treated as gold or silver for zakat; it may be considered a trade good.
This ruling prevents zakat on coins that are not truly gold or silver.
3
Analyze the options and dispute for trade goods
The text presents two methods: paying zakat from the commodity itself (e.g., giving 2.5% of the wheat) or paying its cash value. If paying value, there is a dispute: Imam says value at year-end (day obligation is fixed), Sahibayn say at time of payment. The practical difference arises if price changes between the two dates.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Imam's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبلغ خمسا زكاه بحسابه وما غلب على الغش فكالخالص من النقدين، ولا زكاة الجواهر واللآلي إلا أن يتملكها بنية التجارة كسائر العروض، ولو تمّ الحول على مكيل أو موزون للتجارة فغلا سعره ورخص فأدى من عينه ربع عشره أجزأه، وإن أدى من قيمته تعتبر قيمته يوم الوجوب، وهو تمام الحول عند الإمام، وقالا: يوم الأداء لمصرفها.
And when it reaches five, he gives zakat in proportion. And what is predominated by adulteration is like the pure of the two metals. And there is no zakat on jewels and pearls unless he owns them with the intention of trade, like other trade goods. And if the year passes on a weighed or measured item for trade, and its price rises and falls, and he gives from its substance one-quarter of its tenth, it suffices. And if he gives from its value, its value is considered on the day of obligation, which is the completion of the year according to Imam, and they (Sahibayn) said: on the day of payment to the recipients.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings. First, it reiterates the previous ruling about when the excess reaches five nisabs, zakat is due in proportion. Then it addresses adulterated coins: if a coin is predominantly base metal, it is treated as pure in terms of zakat? Actually 'ma ghalaba 'ala al-ghish fa ka al-khalis min al-naqdain' means that if adulteration dominates, the ruling is like the pure of the two currencies. That likely means that if a gold or silver coin is mixed with other metals, and the pure metal is not the majority, it is not considered zakatable as gold or silver; rather it is like a trade good. But the text is brief. Next: there is no zakat on jewels and pearls unless they are owned with the intention of trade, just like other trade goods. So personal jewelry is not zakatable. Then a key ruling: if a person has a weighed or measured commodity for trade, and its price fluctuates during the year, he has two options for paying zakat: he can pay from the commodity itself (e.g., giving 1/10 of 1/4, i.e., 2.5% of the actual commodity), which is permissible. Or he can pay the cash value. If he pays the value, there is a dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa says the value should be assessed at the end of the year (day of obligation), while the Sahibayn say it should be assessed at the time of actual payment to the poor. The practical difference: if the price increases between the year-end and payment, the Sahibayn require paying zakat on the higher value.

Key Terms
غش (ghish): Adulteration or impurity in gold/silver coins.
عروض التجارة (urud al-tijarah): Goods purchased for trade; subject to zakat on value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a trader has wheat for trade and its price increases between year-end and payment, what value is used for zakat?
A) Value at year-end
B) Value at payment
C) Average of both
D) Lowest value during the year

Key Takeaway
Zakat on trade goods is valued at year-end (mufta bihi), and personal jewelry is exempt from zakat.

Loss of Zakatable Wealth After the Year and Its Effect on Obligation
Ruling on loss of wealth after zakat becomes due, with ikhtilaf on allocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on loss of zakatable wealth after the year and apply Imam Abu Hanifa's method of allocating partial loss to afw and nisabs.

Core Idea
Loss after the year without negligence drops zakat; partial loss is first allocated to the exempt surplus (afw).

This lesson addresses a critical scenario: what happens if your zakatable wealth is destroyed after the year has passed but before you have paid? Does the obligation remain? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer that distinguishes between negligence and non-negligence. Moreover, if only part of the wealth is lost, the method of allocation affects how much zakat remains due. We will explore the ruling and the internal Hanafi disagreement on how to allocate partial loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Read the first line: 'ولا يضمن الزكاة مفرط غير متلف فهلاك المال بعد الحول يسقط الواجب'. What is the rule for loss after the year?
Key: negligence vs non-negligence.
2
Distinguish negligent vs non-negligent loss
The passage says 'مفرط غير متلف' – if the owner is not negligent and does not destroy, they are not liable. If they are negligent or destroy, they remain liable.
3
Understand the allocation method for partial loss
The commentary gives an example: three nisabs plus some afw. The loss is first applied to afw, then to the smallest nisab, then next, etc. This preserves obligation on the larger/earlier nisabs.
This is Imam Abu Hanifa's method.
4
Compare the three Hanafi positions
Imam: loss to afw then nisabs sequentially. Abu Yusuf: loss to nisabs proportionally after afw. Muhammad: loss to both afw and nisabs without order.
The passage explicitly mentions these views.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The text states 'هذا قول الإمام' and implies his view is relied upon. Confirm from later authorities that Imam's method is mufta bihi.
Zahir al-Riwayah supports Imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن الزكاة مفرط غير متلف فهلاك المال بعد الحول يسقط الواجب، وهلاك البعض حصته، ويصرف الهالك إلى العفو، ولو أتلفه يضمن. ويصرف الهالك إلخ : أي لو كان عنده ثلاث نصب مثلاً، وشيء زائد مما لا يبلغ نصاباً رابعاً، فهلك بعض ذلك، رف الهالك إلى العفو أولاً، فإن كان الهالك بقدر العفو يبقى الواجب عليه في ثلاث نصب بتمامه، وإن زاد يصرف الهالك إلى نصاب يليه أي إلى النصاب الثالث ويزكي عن النصابين فإن زاد الهالك على النصاب الثالث يصرف الزائد إلى النصاب الثاني، وهكذا إلى أن ينتهي إلى الأول، ثم إن هذا قول الإمام اليه، وعند أبي يوسف الله: يصرف الهالك بعد العفو الأول إلى النصب شائعاً، وعند محمد إلى العفو والنصب، فلو هلك بعد الحول أربعون =
And a negligent person who does not destroy is not liable for zakat. So the destruction of wealth after the year nullifies the obligation. And the destruction of part [nullifies] its portion. And the destroyed [portion] is allocated to the afw (exempt amount). And if he destroys it, he is liable. 'And the destroyed is allocated to the afw' etc. meaning if one has three nisabs for example and some extra that does not reach a fourth nisab, and some of that is destroyed, the destroyed is first allocated to the afw. If the destroyed is equal to the afw, the obligation upon him remains for the three nisabs completely. If it exceeds, the destroyed is allocated to the next nisab, i.e., to the third nisab, and he gives zakat on the two nisabs. If the destroyed exceeds the third nisab, the excess is allocated to the second nisab, and so on until reaching the first. Then this is the saying of Imam [Abu Hanifa]. And according to Abu Yusuf: after the first afw, the destroyed is allocated to the nisabs proportionally. And according to Muhammad: [allocated] to the afw and the nisabs. So if forty [dirhams] are destroyed after the year...
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where zakatable wealth is lost (destroyed) after the year has completed but before payment. The general rule: if the wealth is lost without the owner's negligence (i.e., they were not at fault), the zakat obligation drops entirely (if all is lost) or proportionally (if part is lost). However, if the owner is negligent or actually destroys the wealth (atlafa), they remain liable. The key point: when partial loss occurs, the lost amount is first allocated to the 'afw' (exempt surplus) as a buffer. If the loss exceeds the afw, it then eats into the nisab(s) starting from the smallest (the third nisab in a case of three nisabs plus afw), then the second, then the first. This method ensures that the zakat obligation is preserved as much as possible for the first nisabs. The passage ends with an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Hanifa's method as described; Abu Yusuf allocates the loss proportionally among all nisabs after the afw; Muhammad allocates loss to both afw and nisabs without order. The text cuts off mid-example (forty dirhams destroyed).

Key Terms
مفرط (mufarrat): One who is negligent in protecting wealth.
هلاك المال (halak al-mal): Loss or destruction of wealth.
العفو (al-afw): Exempt surplus between nisabs; used as buffer in loss allocation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone has 400 dirhams (two nisabs) and 50 dirhams extra (afw), and 60 dirhams are destroyed after the year, what remains subject to zakat according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) 400 dirhams
B) 200 dirhams
C) 390 dirhams
D) 50 dirhams

Key Takeaway
Wealth lost after the year without negligence cancels zakat; partial loss is first absorbed by the exempt surplus (afw) to preserve the obligation on the full nisabs.

The Nisab of Five: Zakat Trigger
Ruling on nisab threshold for zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum nisab for silver and explain how the phrase 'five' (khamsan) triggers zakat.

Core Idea
If your wealth reaches the nisab of five awaq, zakat becomes obligatory on the entire amount.

The text opens with a concise rule: when wealth reaches a specific threshold, zakat is due. The phrase 'five' here refers to five awaq of silver (200 dirhams) or five camels. Understanding this trigger is foundational for zakat calculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab threshold
Recognize that 'khamsan' (five) refers to five awaq of silver (200 dirhams) or five camels. This is the minimum amount that makes zakat wajib.
The exact nisab for gold is 20 mithqal, but the text implies the general rule of five awaq for silver.
2
Apply the calculation
Once the nisab is reached, zakat is due on the full amount, not just the excess. The phrase 'bi hisabihi' indicates proportional calculation on the entire wealth.
3
Distinguish from other nisab amounts
Note that different assets have different nisab. For silver, it is 200 dirhams; for gold, 20 mithqal; for camels, five. This ruling focuses on the threshold itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبلغ خمسا زكاه بحسابه وما غلب على الغش فكالخالص من النقدين، ولا زكاة الجواهر واللآلي إلا أن يتملكها بنية التجارة كسائر العروض
And if it reaches five, he gives zakat on it accordingly; and whatever dominates in adulteration is like the pure of the two metals; and there is no zakat on jewels and pearls unless one owns them with intention of trade like other trade goods.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage states several zakat rulings. First, the nisab of five: for gold, five awaq (200 dirhams) or five camels? Actually 'khamsa' here refers to five awaq of silver (200 dirhams) or five camels? The text says 'balagha khamsan' meaning if it reaches five (awaq of silver or five camels), he pays zakat accordingly. Second, if a metal object is mixed with another metal, the ruling follows the dominant metal—if gold dominates, it is treated as gold; if silver, as silver. Third, jewels and pearls are not subject to zakat unless they are purchased with the intention of trade, in which case they are treated like other trade goods ('urud) and zakat is due on their value at the end of the year.

Key Terms
خمسا (khamsan): Refers to the nisab of five (e.g., five awaq of silver or five camels).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the nisab for silver in the Hanafi school?
A) 100 dirhams
B) 200 dirhams
C) 20 mithqal
D) 5 camels

Key Takeaway
Zakat becomes wajib once wealth reaches the nisab of five; the entire wealth is then assessed.

Zakat on Mixed Metals: The Dominant Element Rule
Ruling on mixed metals for zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that zakat on mixed metal objects follows the dominant metal.

Core Idea
An object's zakat status is determined by its predominant metal, not the minor impurity.

The second ruling addresses objects made from a mixture of gold, silver, or other metals. The Hanafi position is clear: if gold is the dominant element, the object is treated as gold for zakat; if silver dominates, as silver. This simplifies evaluation of mixed items.

Guided Path
1
Determine the dominant metal
Assess the composition: if gold constitutes the majority by weight, the object is considered gold; if silver is majority, it is silver. If neither dominates, further rulings apply.
The text says 'ma ghalaba' — whatever dominates.
2
Apply the corresponding zakat ruling
If the object is treated as gold, zakat is due only if its weight reaches 20 mithqal; if silver, then 200 dirhams nisab applies. Zakat is 2.5% of the value.
3
Note the exception for equal mixtures
If the metals are equal, the object is treated like other trade goods ('urud) and zakat is due on its value at year-end, regardless of nisab of individual metals.
This is derived from later Hanafi texts; the matn itself does not detail equal mixtures.

Source Text

وبلغ خمسا زكاه بحسابه وما غلب على الغش فكالخالص من النقدين، ولا زكاة الجواهر واللآلي إلا أن يتملكها بنية التجارة كسائر العروض
And if it reaches five, he gives zakat on it accordingly; and whatever dominates in adulteration is like the pure of the two metals; and there is no zakat on jewels and pearls unless one owns them with intention of trade like other trade goods.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage states several zakat rulings. First, the nisab of five: for gold, five awaq (200 dirhams) or five camels? Actually 'khamsa' here refers to five awaq of silver (200 dirhams) or five camels? The text says 'balagha khamsan' meaning if it reaches five (awaq of silver or five camels), he pays zakat accordingly. Second, if a metal object is mixed with another metal, the ruling follows the dominant metal—if gold dominates, it is treated as gold; if silver, as silver. Third, jewels and pearls are not subject to zakat unless they are purchased with the intention of trade, in which case they are treated like other trade goods ('urud) and zakat is due on their value at the end of the year.

Key Terms
الغش (al-ghish): Adulteration or impurity in a metal; the ruling follows the dominant element.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Zakat on Jewels and Pearls: Trade Intention Required
Ruling on gems for zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when jewels and pearls are zakatable and when they are not.

Core Idea
Jewels and pearls are not zakatable unless owned with the intention of trade, making them akin to trade goods.

The final ruling clarifies a common point of confusion: precious stones do not automatically attract zakat. Only when they are held as inventory for sale (tijarah) do they become zakatable, following the rules for trade goods. Personal adornment items are exempt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asset type
The ruling applies specifically to jewels (jawahir) and pearls (la'ali). Other precious stones like rubies or diamonds are included by analogy.
2
Determine intention of ownership
Ask whether the owner intends to sell them (trade) or keep them for personal use (adornment, investment without sale intention). Only trade intention makes zakat wajib.
The passage says 'illa an yamtalikaha bi niyat al-tijarah' — unless owned with intention of trade.
3
Apply the ruling
If intended for trade, they are treated like other trade goods: zakat is due on their market value at year-end if the total value of all trade goods reaches the nisab. If for personal use, no zakat.

Source Text

And if it reaches five, he gives zakat on it accordingly; and whatever dominates in adulteration is like the pure of the two metals; and there is no zakat on jewels and pearls unless one owns them with intention of trade like other trade goods.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage states several zakat rulings. First, the nisab of five: for gold, five awaq (200 dirhams) or five camels? Actually 'khamsa' here refers to five awaq of silver (200 dirhams) or five camels? The text says 'balagha khamsan' meaning if it reaches five (awaq of silver or five camels), he pays zakat accordingly. Second, if a metal object is mixed with another metal, the ruling follows the dominant metal—if gold dominates, it is treated as gold; if silver, as silver. Third, jewels and pearls are not subject to zakat unless they are purchased with the intention of trade, in which case they are treated like other trade goods ('urud) and zakat is due on their value at the end of the year.

Key Terms

اللآلي (al-la'ali): Pearls.

Zakat Valuation Date: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on valuation date for zakat payable from trade goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the valuation date for zakat on trade goods and explain the practical consequence of their disagreement.

Core Idea
Zakat valuation: Abu Hanifa uses the end of haul; Sahibayn use the payment date — the difference matters when prices change.

This lesson examines a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school about when to value trade goods for zakat. The ruling affects the amount owed when prices fluctuate after the year completes. The passage presents two distinct positions from the school's founding imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The ruling applies to zakat on trade goods (makilat/mawazin) when the owner pays in cash equivalent rather than from the goods themselves.
If paying from the goods, valuation is not an issue.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's Position
Imam holds that valuation is on the day zakat becomes wajib, i.e., completion of the haul (end of lunar year).
This is based on qiyas: the obligation is fixed at that moment.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that valuation is on the day of actual payment to the recipients.
They consider the fulfillment of the obligation as the relevant time.
4
Determine the Practical Difference (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
If the price rises after the haul ends, Abu Hanifa requires zakat on the lower value; Sahibayn require it on the higher value. If prices fall, the opposite holds.
The dispute only arises when payment is delayed and prices change.
5
Note the Absence of Tarjih
This passage merely records both views without giving preference. Later Hanafi works sometimes prefer one view, but the text itself does not settle it.
Understanding both positions is essential for comprehensive fiqh knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو تمّ الحول على مكيل أو موزون فغلا سعره ورخص فأدى من عينه ربع عشره أجزأه، وإن أدى من قيمته تعتبر قيمته يوم الوجوب، وهو تمام الحول عند الإمام، وقالا: يوم الأداء لمصرفها
And if the year completes on a commodity measured by volume or weight, and its price rises and falls, and he pays from its substance one-tenth of one-fourth (i.e., 2.5%), it suffices him. But if he pays from its value, its value is considered on the day of obligation, which is the completion of the haul according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: on the day of payment to its recipients.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the valuation date for zakat on trade goods (mawazin or makilat). When a merchant has goods whose price fluctuates during the year, and he pays zakat in cash equivalent rather than from the goods themselves, the value is determined differently. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the value should be taken on the day the zakat became wajib, i.e., at the completion of one lunar year (hawl). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that the value should be taken on the day of actual payment to the recipients. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is clear: if the price rose between the end of the haul and the payment, Abu Hanifa requires zakat on the lower value, while the Sahibayn require it on the higher value.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): The point at which zakat becomes obligatory, i.e., completion of one lunar year.
الحول (al-hawl): The lunar year period for zakat on trade goods.
الأداء (al-ada): The actual payment of zakat to the recipients.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when is the value of zakatable trade goods assessed?
A) At the time of purchase.
B) At the completion of the haul.
C) At the time of payment to the poor.
D) At the time of sale.

Key Takeaway
The valuation date for zakat on trade goods in cash is disputed within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa uses the end of the haul, while Sahibayn use the payment date.

Allocation of Lost Zakatable Wealth Among Multiple Nisabs: Intra-Hanafi Views
Intra-Hanafi dispute on allocation of loss among multiple nisabs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for loss of wealth after haul and apply the three intra-Hanafi allocation methods to a multi-nisab scenario.

Core Idea
Loss after haul drops zakat; but if multiple nisabs, how to allocate loss differs among Hanafi imams.

Zakat becomes obligatory when a full lunar year (haul) passes on wealth reaching the nisab. But what if some of that wealth is destroyed after the haul but before payment? This passage from Nur al-Idah explains that the obligation drops for lost wealth, provided the owner was not the cause. When the owner has multiple nisabs with some surplus (afw), the imams disagree on how to allocate the loss. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens our grasp of zakat accounting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
State the general principle: destruction of zakatable wealth after the haul drops the obligation for the lost portion, unless the owner was negligent and caused the loss.
Key: 'mufarrit ghayr mutlif' – negligent but not destructive.
2
Recognize the surplus (afw) and multiple nisabs
Notice that the owner may have surplus wealth (below the next nisab) alongside multiple full nisabs. The question is: which portion of the wealth is considered lost first?
Surplus is called al-afw – wealth that does not reach the next nisab threshold.
3
Learn Imam Abu Hanifa's method
Abu Hanifa says: allocate the loss first to the surplus (afw). If the loss exceeds the surplus, then allocate to the nearest nisab, and so on. This sequential method protects higher nisabs from loss proportion.
The loss is applied stepwise: afw → first nisab → second nisab, etc.
4
Learn the Sahibayn's methods
Abu Yusuf allocates the loss proportionally among all nisabs after the first surplus. Muhammad allocates the loss to both the surplus and the nisabs together (presumably in proportion to their amounts).
These methods differ in how they distribute the loss across the wealth categories.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Take a scenario: 100 dirhams surplus, 200 dirhams in first nisab, 200 in second nisab. Loss = 150 dirhams. Compute the remaining obligation under each method.
This shows the practical impact of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يضمن الزكاة مفرط غير متلف فهلاك المال بعد الحول يسقط الواجب، وهلاك البعض حصته، ويصرف الهالك إلى العفو، فإن لم يجاوزه فالواجب على حاله، فإن جاوزه صرف إلى نصاب يليه، ويسقط من الثاني بحسابه، وهكذا. وهذا قول الإمام، وعند أبي يوسف يصرف الهالك بعد العفو الأول إلى النصب شائعاً، وعند محمد إلى العفو والنصب
And one who is negligent but not destructive does not guarantee the zakat; thus destruction of the wealth after the haul drops the obligation, and destruction of part drops its portion. And the destroyed portion is allocated to the surplus (al-afw), and if it does not exceed it, the obligation remains as is; if it exceeds, it is allocated to the next nisab, and a proportional amount from the second drops, and so on. This is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf, after the first surplus, the destroyed portion is distributed proportionally among the nisabs. According to Muhammad, it is allocated to the surplus and the nisabs.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage discusses what happens if wealth is lost after the zakat obligation has been incurred (after the haul) but before payment. If the owner was negligent (mufarrit) but did not cause the destruction, he does not guarantee the zakat—i.e., the obligation drops for the lost wealth. If part is lost, only that part's zakat drops. The passage then details a method for allocating the loss when there are multiple nisabs and some surplus (afw: wealth below the next nisab). Abu Hanifa's method: first allocate the loss to the surplus, then to the nearest nisab, and so forth. Abu Yusuf distributes the loss proportionally among all nisabs after the first surplus. Muhammad allocates the loss to both the surplus and the nisabs together.

Key Terms
العفو (al-afw): Surplus wealth that does not reach the next nisab threshold.
الحول (al-hawl): The lunar year that must pass for zakat to become obligatory.
النصاب (al-nisab): The minimum threshold of wealth that triggers zakat obligation.
مفرط (mufarrit): One who is negligent but did not directly cause destruction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when wealth is lost after haul and there is surplus (afw) and multiple nisabs, how is the loss allocated?
A) Proportionally to all nisabs.
B) First to the surplus, then to the nearest nisab.
C) Equally between surplus and nisabs.
D) Only to the smallest nisab.

Key Takeaway
Loss of zakatable wealth after the haul drops the obligation; but with multiple nisabs, the allocation method varies among Hanafi imams, affecting the remaining zakat due.

Forcible Collection and Bequest of Unpaid Zakat
Ruling on forcible collection and bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forcible collection of zakat and explain the condition under which unpaid zakat can be taken from the deceased's estate.

Core Idea
Zakat is not forcibly collected; unpaid zakat is taken only if bequeathed, and then from one-third of the estate.

This lesson addresses two practical questions: Can the state forcibly collect zakat from a person who has not paid? And if a person dies without paying zakat, can it be taken from the estate? The Hanafi school gives a clear response based on the principle that zakat is a religious obligation, not a debt enforced by compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that zakat is not taken forcibly (jabran) from a living person. Identify this as a prohibition on forcible collection.
The ruling applies to the collector, not the payer.
2
Analyze the exception for the deceased
If a person dies without paying zakat, it can only be taken from the estate if the deceased specifically bequeathed it. The bequest is limited to one-third of the estate.
This follows the general rule that bequests cannot exceed one-third without heir consent.
3
Understand the legal consequence
Without a bequest, the unpaid zakat is not taken from the estate; the obligation is not transferred to heirs as a debt.
This differs from unpaid debts, which are taken from the estate without a bequest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم يجاوزه فالواجب على حاله، ولا تؤخذ الزكاة جبراً، ولا من تركته إلا أن يوصي بها، فتكون من ثلثه ويجيز أبو يوسف الحيلة لدفع وجوب الزكاة، وكرهها محمد
And if it does not exceed it, the obligation remains as it is. And zakat is not taken forcibly, nor from his estate unless he bequeaths it, then it is from one-third. And Abu Yusuf permits the legal trick (hila) to repel the obligation of zakat, and Muhammad disapproved of it.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on loss allocation and then addresses two issues. First, zakat is not to be forcibly collected from a person who has not paid; nor is it taken from his estate after death unless he specifically bequeaths it, in which case it is limited to one-third of the estate (the general will rule). Second, it mentions a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding legal tricks (hiyal) to avoid zakat. Abu Yusuf permits using a legal trick before the obligation becomes due (e.g., giving away nisab before haul completion) to escape zakat, while Muhammad disapproves of such tricks as makruh. The passage indicates that the relied-upon position is that such tricks are disapproved.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem to avoid an obligation before it becomes due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Automatically, like debts.
B) Only if the deceased bequeathed it.
C) Only if heirs agree.
D) It is never taken from the estate.

Key Takeaway
Zakat is not forcibly collected; unpaid zakat is taken from the estate only if the deceased bequeaths it, and then from one-third.

Legal Tricks (Hila) to Avoid Zakat: The Hanafi Internal Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on legal tricks to avoid zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on using legal tricks (hiyal) to avoid zakat and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A legal trick to avoid zakat is makruh — Muhammad disapproves, Abu Yusuf allows.

This lesson explores a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the use of legal stratagems (hiyal) to avoid the obligation of zakat before it becomes due. Abu Yusuf sees no harm in such tricks, while Muhammad considers them disapproved. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens our grasp of how the school balances legal formality with ethical consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is using a legal trick (hila) to avoid zakat before it becomes wajib, e.g., giving away or decreasing the nisab before the haul completes.
The hila is permissible in contract law but here applied to ibadat.
2
State Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf permits the hila, meaning it is not sinful and the person is not liable for zakat.
His basis: the obligation has not yet become due; the hila is simply a precaution.
3
State Muhammad's view
Muhammad disapproves of the hila, considering it makruh (disliked). He argues it circumvents a religious duty.
The passage uses 'karihaha' indicating strong disapproval.
4
Analyze the likely basis of each view
Abu Yusuf emphasizes formal criteria: no obligation yet, so no sin. Muhammad emphasizes intention and the spirit of the law: deliberately avoiding worship is blameworthy.
The difference reflects a tension between literal and ethical readings.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Although the text does not give tarjih, later Hanafi works often prefer Muhammad's view as more precautionary. Both views have weight.
The mufta bihi is typically the view of Muhammad (disapproval).

Source Text

And if it does not exceed it, the obligation remains as it is. And zakat is not taken forcibly, nor from his estate unless he bequeaths it, then it is from one-third. And Abu Yusuf permits the legal trick (hila) to repel the obligation of zakat, and Muhammad disapproved of it.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on loss allocation and then addresses two issues. First, zakat is not to be forcibly collected from a person who has not paid; nor is it taken from his estate after death unless he specifically bequeaths it, in which case it is limited to one-third of the estate (the general will rule). Second, it mentions a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding legal tricks (hiyal) to avoid zakat. Abu Yusuf permits using a legal trick before the obligation becomes due (e.g., giving away nisab before haul completion) to escape zakat, while Muhammad disapproves of such tricks as makruh. The Ruling on Hilah to Avoid Zakat: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Rulings on legal tricks to escape zakat obligation with Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on using hilah to avoid zakat before year-end and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Hilah before obligation is permissible; after obligation, it is makruh by consensus.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the use of legal stratagems (hilah) to escape the obligation of zakat before the end of the lunar year. The passage presents the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, with the relied-upon position being that of Abu Yusuf, but with important nuances regarding intention and context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What act is being discussed? Using a legal trick to avoid zakat before the year completes. Determine the hukm according to each imam.
The act is the hilah itself, not the underlying transaction.
2
Locate the Dispute
Who agrees and disagrees? Abu Yusuf permits it; Muhammad dislikes it. The text also shows a consensus on certain conditions.
Remember that 'al-ashah' indicates the preferred view.
3
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf
What is the practical difference? A person may sell nisab a day before year-end; Abu Yusuf says no karaah, Muhammad says makruh.
The consequence affects whether the action is considered blameworthy.
4
Apply the Conditions
Note the exceptions: selling for genuine expenses is not makruh by consensus. Using tricks to drop an obligation or out of miserliness is makruh by consensus.
Intention matters: 'bukhla' (miserliness) triggers consensus karaah.
5
Derive the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon view: Abu Yusuf's position is al-ashah. Hilah before wajib is not makruh unless done out of miserliness.
This ruling applies to any legal trick to avoid zakat before year-end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة مسائل شتی فإن لم يجاوزه فالواجب على حاله، ولا تؤخذ الزكاة جبراً، ولا من تركته إلا أن يوصي بها، فتكون من ثلثه ويجيز أبو يوسف الحيلة لدفع وجوب الزكاة، وكرهها محمد من ثمانين شاة، تجب شاة كاملة عندهما، وعند محمد نصف شاة، ولو هلك خمسة عشر من أربعين بعيراً تجب بنت مخاض؛ لما أن الإمام يصرف الهالك إلى العفو، ثم إلى نصاب يليه ثم وثم وعند أبي يوسف خمسة وعشرون جزء من ستة وثلاثين جزءً من بنت مخاض؛ لما مر أنه يصرف الهالك بعد العفو الأول إلى النصب، وعند محمد نصف بنت لبون وثمنها؛ لما مر أنه يعلق الزكاة بالنصاب والعفو رد المحتار بحذف: ٢٨٣/٢] ويجيز إلخ : قال في "البحر": اعلم أنه لو وهب النصاب في خلال الحول، ثم تم الحول وهو عند الموهوب له، ثم رجع للواهب بعد الحول بقضاء أو بغيره فلا زكاة على واحد منهما، كما في "الخانية"، وهي من حيل إسقاط الزكاة قبل الوجوب، وفي "المعراج : ولو باع السوائم قبل تمام الحول بيوم فراراً عن الوجوب، قال محمد: يكره، وقال أبو يوسف لا يكره، وهو الأصح، ولو باعها للنفقة لا يكره بالإجماع، ولو احتال لإسقاط الواجب يكره بالإجماع، ولو فرّ من الوجوب بخلاً لا تأثماً، يكره بالإجماع. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ۷۱۸]
Chapter of Zakat: Miscellaneous Issues. If he does not exceed it, the obligation remains as it is. Zakat is not taken by force, nor from his estate unless he bequeaths it, then it is from his third. Abu Yusuf permits the legal trick (hilah) to repel the obligation of zakat, while Muhammad dislikes it. From eighty sheep, a whole sheep is obligatory according to them both, while according to Muhammad half a sheep. If fifteen out of forty camels perish, a bint makhad is obligatory because the imam apportions the perished to the surplus, then to the next nisab, then and then. According to Abu Yusuf, twenty-five thirty-sixths of a bint makhad, because he apportions the perished after the first surplus to the nisabs. According to Muhammad, half a bint labun and its eighth, because he attaches zakat to the nisab and surplus. Radd al-Muhtar [citation]. And more: It is stated in al-Bahr: Know that if one gifts the nisab during the year, then the year completes while it is with the donee, then the donor takes it back after the year by judgment or otherwise, no zakat is due from either, as in al-Khaniyyah. This is among the tricks to drop zakat before it becomes obligatory. In al-Mi'raj: If one sells grazing livestock a day before the year completes fleeing from obligation, Muhammad says it is disliked, Abu Yusuf says it is not disliked, and that is the most correct. If he sells for expenses, it is not disliked by consensus. If he uses a trick to drop an obligation, it is disliked by consensus. If he flees from obligation out of miserliness without sinning, it is disliked by consensus. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 718]
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling on using legal tricks (hilal) to avoid the obligation of zakat. The text first states the default rule: if the nisab is not exceeded, the obligation stands, and zakat cannot be taken forcibly or from the estate unless bequeathed. Then a dispute is introduced: Abu Yusuf permits the hilah to avoid zakat before its obligation becomes due, while Muhammad dislikes it. An example is given for sheep: from eighty sheep, a whole sheep is due according to Abu Yusuf and the Imam (Abu Hanifa?), but Muhammad says half. For camels that perish, the method of apportioning loss affects the calculation. The text then quotes al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, mentioning a scenario where gifting nisab during the year and then taking it back after the year avoids zakat. Further, from al-Mi'raj, the ruling on selling livestock a day before year-end: Muhammad says makruh, Abu Yusuf says not makruh, and the latter is the most correct (al-ashah). However, if the sale is for genuine expenses, it is not makruh by consensus. Using tricks to drop an obligation is makruh by consensus, and fleeing out of miserliness without intending sin is also makruh by consensus. The passage thus provides a nuanced view: hilah before obligation is permissible according to Abu Yusuf and the relied-upon view, but intention and context matter; it becomes makruh when done to evade a duty out of miserliness.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal stratagem to avoid a legal obligation, in this case zakat, before it becomes due.
السوائم (al-sawa'im): Grazing livestock (camels, cattle, sheep/goats) subject to zakat if they reach nisab and are free-ranging.
الأصح (al-ashah): The most correct view in a given ikhtilaf, indicating the preferred position for fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the ruling that 'using a trick to drop an obligation is makruh by consensus' apply?
A) When the trick is used before the zakat year completes.
B) When the trick is used to drop an obligation that has already become wajib.
C) When the trick is done out of miserliness.
D) When the trick involves selling livestock.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, using a legal trick to avoid zakat before the obligation is due is permissible according to the relied-upon view, but becomes disliked if done out of miserliness or after the obligation is fixed.

Definition and Conditions of Zakat Recipients (Masrif al-Zakat)
Defining the eight categories of zakat recipients with Hanafi qualifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define each of the eight categories of zakat recipients according to the Hanafi school and identify the conditions for their eligibility.

Core Idea
Zakat must be given to specific Quranic recipients, each with distinct conditions and qualifications.

This lesson unpacks the eight categories of zakat disbursement (masrif) as defined in the Hanafi school, based on the passage from Nur al-Idah and its commentaries. You will learn the precise definitions of the poor (faqir), needy (miskin), debtors, wayfarers, and those in the path of Allah (fi sabilillah), including an intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of the latter. Understanding these categories ensures correct disbursement of zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic foundation
Recognize that the eight categories are listed in Surah al-Tawbah 9:60. The passage elaborates on each with Hanafi-specific conditions.
The category of mu'allafatu qulubihim is abrogated by ijma.
2
Define the poor (faqir) and needy (miskin)
Note the Hanafi distinction: faqir owns property below nisab or nisab consumed by basic needs; miskin has nothing. This reverses the order common in some other schools.
The passage emphasizes that faqir includes those whose nisab is consumed by needs.
3
Define the mukatab and madyun
Mukatab (slave under contract) is helped regardless of master's wealth; madyun (debtor) must not own nisab or earnings beyond his debt. The debtor's eligibility depends on the debtor's solvency.
A debtor with an insolvent debtor is treated like a wayfarer.
4
Analyze the interpretation of 'fi sabilillah'
Compare Abu Yusuf's view (stranded fighters/hajj) with Muhammad's broader view (including students and Quran carriers). Both are accepted; the difference is interpretive.
The text states Abu Yusuf's interpretation as his choice but does not reject Muhammad's.
5
Define ibn al-sabil and note the omission
Ibn al-sabil is a traveler cut off from his property. Even if rich at home, he is poor in hand. The passage also notes mu'allafa is omitted due to abrogation.
Borrowing is better than accepting charity for a wayfarer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
