(البحر الرائق منقطع الغزاة: بفتح الطاء، والغزاة جمع الغازي، أي الذين عجزوا عن اللحوق بجيش الإسلام لفقرهم بهلاك النفقة أو الدابة أو غيرهما فتحل لهم الصدقة وإن كانوا كاسبين؛ إذ الكسب يقعدهم عن الجهاد، وهم بالاستحقاق أرسخ وأولى؛ لزيادة الحاجة بالفقر والانقطاع، وهذا التفسير اختيار أبي يوسف. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٧١٩] الحاج أي منقطع الحاج، وهو قول محمد وقيل طلبة العلم، وقيل: حملة القرآن الفقراء، والخلاف بين أبي يوسف ومحمد إنما هو في تفسير الآية، لا في جواز الدفع إلى الجميع بشرطه [حاشية الطحطاوي بتصرف: ٧٢] وابن السبيل: هو المنقطع عن . ماله لبعده عنه. والسبيل الطريق، فكل من : يكون مسافراً يسمى ابن السبيل، وهو غني حتى تجب الزكاة في ماله ويؤمر بالأداء إذا وصلت إليه يده، وهو فقير يدا حتى تصرف إليه الصدقة في الحال لحاجته. فإن قلت منقطع الغزاة أو الحج إن لم يكن في وطنه مال فهو فقير، وإلا فهو ابن السبيل، فكيف تكون الأقسام سبعة؟ قلت: هو فقير إلا أنه زاد عليه بالانقطاع في عبادة الله تعالى، فكان مغايراً للفقير المطلق الخالي عن والاستقراض لابن السبيل خير من قبول الصدقة ولا يحل له أن يأخذ أكثر من حاجته البحر الرائق بحذف: ٣٨٣/٢]
بمكانه
:
هذا القيد
Chapter of the Disbursement: The disbursement of zakat is to the poor (faqir): he is one who possesses property that does not reach the nisab, nor its value from any type of property, even if it is sound. And if he is poor (faqir) and the needy (miskin): he is one who has nothing. And the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) and the debtor who does not possess a nisab nor an earning. His value in excess over his debt. And for the cause of Allah (fi sabilillah): he is a stranded fighter or pilgrim. And the wayfarer (ibn al-sabil). The mu'allafatu qulubihim [hearts won over]... The chapter: 'Masrif' linguistically means place of turning. Al-Qahistani defined it terminologically: it is a Muslim to whom it is valid in Shariah to give charity to him. He did not restrict it in the book to disbursement of zakat; to include zakat, tithe (ushr), and one-fifth of mines, as indicated in al-Nihayah. And it is recommended to give one-fifth of mines because its disbursement is war booty. He mentioned seven categories and omitted mu'allafatu qulubihim to indicate abrogation by consensus of the Companions. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq] 'That does not reach nisab' means or possesses what reaches nisab but is consumed by his needs. So whoever has either condition is poor. One who has a deferred debt upon someone, if he needs for expenses, it is permissible for him to take from zakat an amount sufficient for him until the term; if the debt is not deferred and the debtor is insolvent, he may take zakat in the most correct opinion because he is like a wayfarer; if the debtor is wealthy and admits [the debt], it is not permissible for him to take zakat. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 719] Al-mukatib: that is, the mukatab is helped in freeing his neck. It is unrestricted, so it includes whether his master is poor or rich, and no difference between minor and adult, contrary to al-Hadadi's restriction to adults. And the debtor: in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah, paying to one who owes debt is better than paying to the poor. (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) 'Stranded fighter': with fath on the ta', and ghuzat is plural of ghazi, i.e., those unable to join the army of Islam due to poverty caused by loss of provisions or mount or other things, so charity is permissible for them even if they are earning because earning would prevent them from jihad, and they are more deserving because of increased need due to poverty and being stranded. This interpretation is the choice of Abu Yusuf. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 719] Al-hajj: i.e., stranded pilgrim. This is the saying of Muhammad. And it is said: seekers of knowledge, and it is said: poor bearers of the Quran. The difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is only in the interpretation of the verse, not in permissibility of giving to all with conditions. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi] Wayfarer: he is one cut off from his property due to distance. Sabii is the road. So everyone who is traveling is called ibn al-sabil. He is rich in the sense that zakat is due on his property at his residence, but he is poor in hand so charity is given to him at the present due to his need. If you say: the stranded fighter or pilgrim, if he has no wealth in his homeland he is poor, otherwise he is a wayfarer; so how are the categories seven? I say: he is poor except that he is increased by being stranded in worship of Allah Almighty, so he is different from the absolute poor free from [residence?]. And borrowing is better for a wayfarer than accepting charity, and it is not permissible for him to take more than his need. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq] This condition. [Mark?]
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the categories of recipients of zakat (masrif). It opens the chapter (bab) on disbursement. It begins by defining the poor (faqir) as someone who does not own nisab or its equivalent, and the needy (miskin) as one who has nothing. It then includes the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) and the debtor (madyun) who does not own nisab or ability to earn beyond his debt. Next it defines 'fi sabilillah' as stranded fighters or pilgrims, and 'ibn al-sabil' as the wayfarer. The text notes that the category of mu'allafatu qulubihim (those whose hearts are to be won) is omitted due to abrogation by ijma. Commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Hashiyat al-Tahtawi elaborates: The definition of faqir includes those whose nisab is consumed by basic needs or who have deferred debt; a debtor with an insolvent debtor is treated like a wayfarer and may take zakat. The mukatab is helped regardless of master's wealth or age. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of 'fi sabilillah': Abu Yusuf views it as stranded fighters/hajj, while Muhammad includes students of knowledge and Quran carriers, but both agree that if conditions are met, it is permissible to give to any. The wayfarer (ibn al-sabil) is defined as someone cut off from his property while traveling; though he may be rich at home, he is considered poor in hand. The seven categories are explained: stranded fighters/pilgrims who have no home property are poor, while those who have property are ibn al-sabil, yet they still differ due to the worship context. The passage ends by noting that for ibn al-sabil, borrowing is better than accepting charity and he should not take more than his need.

Key Terms
الفقير (al-faqir): One who owns property that does not reach the nisab, or owns nisab but it is consumed by basic needs.
المسكين (al-miskin): One who possesses nothing.
في سبيل الله (fi sabilillah): Stranded fighter or pilgrim according to Abu Yusuf; also includes students and Quran carriers according to Muhammad.
ابن السبيل (ibn al-sabil): A traveler cut off from his property due to distance; considered poor in the present context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a debtor who has a debt on an insolvent person taking zakat?
A) It is not permissible in any case.
B) It is permissible in the most correct opinion because he is like a wayfarer.
C) It is permissible only if the debt is deferred.
D) It is prohibited because he is not poor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines zakat recipients with specific conditions, and the category 'fi sabilillah' is interpreted broadly within the school to include students and Quran carriers in addition to fighters and pilgrims.

Ineligible Zakat Recipients (Hanafi Ruling)
States general categories of invalid zakat recipients

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five categories of people to whom zakat cannot be given and explain the consequence of giving to them.

Core Idea
Zakat given to a disbeliever, a rich person, a rich child, or Banu Hashim is invalid and must be repeated.

The Quran specifies eight categories of zakat recipients, but the Hanafi school excludes certain individuals even if they fall under those categories. This lesson clarifies who cannot receive zakat and the ruling on the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Zakat can be given to any of the eight Quranic categories, and the payer may give to one or all.
2
Recognize the exclusions
The text lists five groups to whom it is not valid to give zakat: a kafir, a rich person (owning nisab surplus), a rich child, Banu Hashim, and their mawali.
3
Understand the consequence
Giving zakat to any of these groups invalidates the zakat; it must be repeated.
4
Note the difference of opinion
Al-Tahawi allowed giving to Banu Hashim, but the relied-upon view in the school is prohibition.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مصرف الزكاة وهو من له مال في وطنه، وليس معه مال والعامل عليها يعطى قدر ما يسعه وأعوانه. وللمزكي الدفع إلى كل الأصناف، وله الاقتصار على واحد مع وجود الأصناف. ولا يصح دفعها لكافر، وغني يملك نصابا أو ما يساوي قيمته من أي مال كان فاضل عن حوائجه الأصلية، وطفل غني، وبني هاشم ومواليهم. واختار الطحاوي جواز دفعها لبني هاشم،
The expenditure of zakat: it is [for] one who has wealth in his homeland but does not have [it] with him, and the collector is given an amount that suffices him and his assistants. And for the zakat-payer, it is permissible to give to all categories, and he may limit to one category even if other categories exist. And it is not valid to give it to a disbeliever, to a rich person who owns a nisab or its equivalent in any wealth that is surplus over his basic needs, to a rich child, or to Banu Hashim and their mawali. And al-Tahawi chose the permissibility of giving it to Banu Hashim.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing those who cannot receive zakat. The default rule is that zakat is given to the eight categories mentioned in the Quran, but here it clarifies who is excluded. The payer may give to all or one category. The excluded are: a kafir (since zakat is a purification for Muslims), a rich person possessing nisab surplus to basic needs, a rich child (even if under guardianship), Banu Hashim (the clan of the Prophet, as zakat is impure for them), and their freed slaves (mawali). Al-Tahawi's preferred opinion allows giving to Banu Hashim, but the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is prohibition.

Key Terms
مصرف الزكاة (masrif al-zakah): The categories of people to whom zakat may be given or the ruling on who is eligible.
كافر (kafir): A non-Muslim; giving zakat to a kafir is invalid.
غني (ghani): A rich person who owns nisab surplus to basic needs; zakat cannot be given to such a person.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum threshold of wealth that triggers zakat liability and defines richness.
بني هاشم (Banu Hashim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him); they are prohibited from receiving zakat.
موالي (mawali): Freed slaves of Banu Hashim; they are also prohibited from receiving zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a valid recipient of zakat?
A) A poor Muslim
B) A rich child
C) A poor relative
D) A non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
Zakat is invalid if given to a disbeliever, a rich person, a rich child, Banu Hashim, or their mawali; it must be repeated.

Definition of Nisab: What Disqualifies a Zakat Recipient
Defines nisab as a disqualifying condition for zakat recipients

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nisab as per the Hanafi school and apply it to determine eligibility for zakat receipt.

Core Idea
Any surplus wealth, growing or not, free from debt, makes one ineligible for zakat.

In the previous lesson, we learned that zakat may only be given to the poor and needy. But what exactly is considered 'rich'? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the term nisab used in the matn, showing that both productive and non-productive wealth, as long as it is surplus to basic needs and free from debt, suffices to make a person ghanī (rich) and therefore ineligible for zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term
The passage defines 'nisab' as used in the ruling that a zakat recipient must not possess nisab.
2
Distinguish the two types of nisab
Note that nisab includes both growing (namī) and non-growing (ghayr nāmī) wealth. This is a general application.
3
Identify the conditions for nisab
The wealth must be free from debt and surplus over basic needs (hāwā’ij aṣliyyah).
These conditions ensure the wealth is truly surplus.
4
Understand the legal effect
Possession of such nisab makes one ghanī, thus preventing him from receiving zakat. It also obligates other financial duties like fitr, udhiyah, and nafaqah.
This further confirms the person's wealth status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نصابا : أطلقه فشمل النصاب النامي السالم من الدين الفاضل عن الحوائج الأصلية، الموجب لكل واجب مالي، والنصاب الذي ليس بنام الفارغ عما ذكر الموجب لثلاثة صدقة الفطر والأضحية ونفقة القريب، فإن كلا منهما لأخذ الزكاة [البحر الرائق: ۳۸۷/۲]
[The term] 'nisab': it was left general, so it includes the growing nisab free from debt, surplus over basic needs, which necessitates every financial obligation, and the nisab that is not growing, free from what was mentioned, which necessitates three things: sadaqat al-fitr, sacrifice (udhiyah), and maintenance of a relative, for each of these [nisabs] prevents taking zakat. [al-Bahr al-Raiq: 2/387]
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'nisab' as used in the earlier ruling. The commentator clarifies that nisab here is general: it includes both productive (namī) and non-productive (ghayr nāmī) wealth. The key condition is that this wealth is surplus to basic needs (hāwā’ij aṣliyyah) and free from debt. A person possessing such nisab is considered rich (ghanī) and cannot receive zakat. Moreover, possessing a non-growing nisab also obligates other financial duties like fitr, udhiyah, and nafaqah of relatives, further indicating wealth.

Key Terms
نصاب نامي (nisab nami): Wealth that grows or increases, such as trade goods, livestock, or money that can be invested.
غني (ghani): Rich person; one who possesses nisab and is therefore ineligible to receive zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person has non-growing savings equal to nisab but no other wealth. Can he receive zakat according to Hanafi?
A) Yes, because it's not growing
B) No, because any nisab disqualifies
C) Yes, if he has debts
D) No, only if he has basic needs

Key Takeaway
Possession of any nisab, whether growing or not, free from debt and surplus to basic needs, disqualifies a person from receiving zakat.

Basic Needs and Zakat Eligibility: The Hanafi Exception
Commentary on the condition 'surplus over basic needs' from al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition that basic needs (al-hawa'ij al-asliyyah) exempt a person from the zakat eligibility requirement of having surplus wealth.

Core Idea
Wealth tied up in basic needs is not 'surplus'—even if it reaches nisab, zakat reception is permissible.

Zakat is given to the poor and needy. But what defines 'need' in Hanafi fiqh? The classical scholars clarified that 'surplus over basic needs' is the key condition. This lesson examines a passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq that sets a crucial boundary: if a person's entire wealth is consumed by essential needs—like a scholar's books or a craftsman's tools—he may still receive zakat despite the assets reaching the nisab threshold. This ruling protects those whose capital is tied up in the necessities of their livelihood or profession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
The lesson focuses on 'fāḍil ‘an al-ḥawā’ij al-aṣliyyah' (surplus over basic needs). Recognize that this phrase conditions zakat eligibility. Note that the passage restricts (qayyadnā) this phrase.
The commentary explicitly states that the condition is restricted to being free of basic needs.
2
Understand the exception
The passage states that if wealth is 'mustaghriqan bihā' (consumed by basic needs), then receiving zakat becomes permissible (ḥallat lahu). The example: a scholar owning books worth nisab. Explain why the books are considered a basic need.
The key is that the wealth is not freely available; it is tied up in essential items.
3
Apply to similar cases
Extend the principle: any professional tool or essential item required for livelihood fulfills this condition. The Hanafi school includes items specific to the person's trade or knowledge.
This is an expansion of basic needs beyond food and clothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاضل إلخ قيدنا بكونه فارغا عن الحوائج الأصلية؛ لأنه لو كان مستغرقا بها حلّت له فتحل لمن ملك كتبا تساوي نصابا وهو من أهلها للحاجة [البحر الرائق بتصرف: ۳۸۷/۲]
[The phrase] 'surplus, etc.': we restricted it to being free of basic needs; because if it is consumed by them, it becomes permissible for him. So it is permissible for one who owns books worth a nisab and he is a person of knowledge due to need. [al-Bahr al-Raiq with adaptation: 2/387]
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This commentary explains the phrase 'fāḍil ‘an al-ḥawā’ij al-aṣliyyah' (surplus over basic needs). If a person's wealth is entirely consumed by his basic needs (e.g., essential tools for his profession, books for a scholar), then he is not considered rich even if the items are worth a nisab. The example given: a scholar who owns books worth a nisab – since the books are a basic need for his work, he may receive zakat. This is a Hanafi ruling that protects those whose wealth is tied up in necessities.

Key Terms
الحوائج الأصلية (al-hawa'ij al-asliyyah): Basic needs such as essential clothing, food, shelter, tools of trade, or books for a scholar.
فاضل (fāḍil): Surplus wealth beyond what is needed for basic needs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A scholar owns books worth $5,000 (nisab) but uses them for teaching. Can he receive zakat?
A) No, because assets reach nisab
B) Yes, because books are a basic need
C) No, if he has other wealth
D) Yes, only if he sells the books

Key Takeaway
Basic needs (al-hawa'ij al-asliyyah) exempt the value of essential items from the zakat eligibility condition of surplus wealth.

Ruling on Giving Zakat to a Rich Child
Defines a rich child and its ineligibility for zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a rich child (tifl ghani) and state that giving zakat to such a person is invalid, and distinguish this from permissible recipients.

Core Idea
A rich child cannot receive zakat even if dependent; adult children and spouses of the rich may be eligible.

This lesson clarifies who qualifies as a 'rich child' in zakat distribution. A minor who owns wealth reaching nisab cannot be given zakat, regardless of guardianship. This contrasts with adult children, fathers, and wives of wealthy individuals, who may be eligible despite the richness of their relative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'tifl ghani' and its scope
The text uses 'child' generally, covering male/female, and those under paternal care or not, according to the correct view. The key is that the child is rich, i.e., owns nisab independently.
Guardianship does not affect the child's own wealth status.
2
State the ruling on giving zakat to a rich child
It is invalid (batil). The text implies prohibition, and al-Bahr al-Raiq confirms this.
Zakat is not discharged if given to a rich child.
3
Understand the contrast with adult children and relatives
Giving to an adult child of a rich person is permissible because the adult is a separate financial entity. Similarly, giving to the father or wife of a rich person is permissible even if they receive maintenance.
The richness of a relative does not automatically make the recipient ineligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطفل غني : أطلق الطفل فشمل الذكر والأنثى، ومن هو في عيال الأب أو لا على الصحيح، وقيد بالطفل؛ لأن الدفع لولد الغني إذا كان كبيرا جائز مطلقا، ولأن الدفع إلى أب الغني وزوجته جائز، سواء فرض لها نفقة أو لا. [البحر الرائق بحذف: ٣٨٩/٢]
And a rich child: the term 'child' was left general so it includes male and female, and one who is under the father's care or not, according to the correct view. And it was restricted to 'child' because giving to a rich person's child if he is an adult is absolutely permissible, and because giving to a rich person's father and his wife is permissible, whether maintenance is specified for her or not. [al-Bahr al-Raiq with deletion: 2/389]
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines 'Ṭifl ghanī' (rich child). 'Child' includes both male and female, and includes those under paternal care and those not, on the correct view. The ruling is that giving zakat to a rich child is invalid. This contrasts with an adult child of a rich parent – that is permissible because the adult child is considered a separate entity. Similarly, giving to the father or wife of a rich person is permissible even if they receive maintenance, because they are separate individuals and may be poor despite the rich relative.

Key Terms
طفل غني (tifl ghanī): A minor child who possesses wealth reaching nisab, regardless of guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A 7-year-old orphan inherits $10,000 (nisab). Can she receive zakat?
A) Yes, because she is a child
B) No, because she is rich
C) Yes, if she has no guardian
D) No, only if she is an adult

Key Takeaway
A minor who owns nisab-level wealth is ineligible for zakat, even if under a rich guardian's care.

Banu Hashim and the Prohibition of Zakat
Explains the ruling and lineage distinctions for Banu Hashim regarding zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to Banu Hashim, distinguish between Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, and explain why the prohibition is permanent.

Core Idea
Banu Hashim can never receive zakat – lineage alone triggers the prohibition.

This passage from Nur al-Idah clarifies a critical rule in zakat distribution: the descendants of Hashim (the Prophet's clan) are permanently prohibited from receiving zakat. The text also explains why the related clan, Banu al-Muttalib, is excluded from this prohibition. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct zakat disbursement and avoiding invalidation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited group
Recognize that Banu Hashim are the descendants of Hashim ibn 'Abd Manaf. The ruling is general, covering all of them, including those who did not support the Prophet (like the offspring of Abu Lahab).
Lineage is decisive, not personal piety.
2
Understand the scope of prohibition
The prohibition includes every Hashimi, even if they become Muslim. The text uses 'ittlaq' (generality) to emphasize that no exception is made based on support or belief.
This refutes the idea that only non-Muslim Hashimis are excluded.
3
Contrast with Banu al-Muttalib
Banu al-Muttalib are permissible recipients of zakat, even though they share the same grandfather 'Abd Manaf. The Prophet differentiated them, so the Hanafi school follows that distinction.
The text explicitly says 'Banu al-Muttalib are not like Banu Hashim'.
4
Analyze the rejection of temporary exception
The text rejects a weak narration that would permit zakat to Banu Hashim temporarily because their compensation (khums) was not reaching them. Instead, the ruling is absolute and not time-bound.
This is based on the principle that the original prohibition remains unless unequivocally abrogated.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that giving zakat to any Hashimi is permanently prohibited. A Hashimi cannot even give zakat to another Hashimi.
Al-Bahr al-Raiq explicitly rejects the contrary narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبني هاشم أي لا يجوز الدفع لهم أطلق في بني هاشم فشمل من كان ناصرا للنبي ومن لم يكن ناصرا له كولد أبي لهب فيدخل من أسلم منهم في حرمة الصدقة؛ لكونه هاشميا، وقيد بـ"بني المطلب تحل لهم الصدقة وليسوا كبني هاشم وإن استووا في القرابة؛ لأن عبد مناف جد النبي ؛ لأنه بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم بن عبد مناف، ولعبد مناف أربعة بنين: هاشم والمطلب ونوفل وعبد شمس. وأطلق الحكم في بني هاشم و لم يقيده بزمان ولا بشخص؛ للإشارة إلى رد رواية أبي عصمة عن الإمام: أنه يجوز الدفع إلى بني هاشم في زمانه؛ لأن عوضها - وهو خمس الخمس - لم يصل إليهم؛ لإهمال الناس أمر الغنائم وعدم إيصالها إلى مستحقها وإذا لم يصل إليهم العوض عادوا إلى المعوض، وللإشارة إلى رد الرواية بأن الهاشمي يجوز له أن يدفع زكاته إلى هاشمي مثله [البحر الرائق بحذف ۳۹۰/۲]
And Banu Hashim: i.e., it is not permissible to give to them. [The term] 'Banu Hashim' was left general so it includes whoever was a supporter of the Prophet and whoever was not, like the son of Abu Lahab. So anyone among them who became Muslim is included in the prohibition of charity because he is a Hashimi. And it was restricted [that] Banu al-Muttalib are permissible for charity and they are not like Banu Hashim even though they are equal in kinship; because 'Abd Manaf is the grandfather of the Prophet, for he is the son of 'Abd al-Muttalib son of Hashim son of 'Abd Manaf, and 'Abd Manaf had four sons: Hashim, al-Muttalib, Nawfal, and 'Abd Shams. And the ruling was left general for Banu Hashim, not restricted by time or person; to indicate the rejection of the narration of Abu 'Isma from the Imam that it is permissible to give to Banu Hashim in his time, because their compensation – which is one-fifth of the fifth – did not reach them due to people neglecting the matter of booty and not delivering it to those entitled; and if compensation does not reach them, they revert to the compensated. And also to indicate rejection of the narration that a Hashimi may give his zakat to a fellow Hashimi. [al-Bahr al-Raiq with deletion: 2/390]
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage expands on the prohibition for Banu Hashim. The prohibition includes all descendants of Hashim, even those who did not support the Prophet (like the offspring of Abu Lahab). Once they become Muslim, the prohibition remains due to lineage. Banu al-Muttalib, however, are an exception – they may receive zakat, as the Prophet made them equal in some respects but not for charity. The ruling is general and not restricted to a specific time, refuting a weak narration that the prohibition was only for the Prophet's time because the compensation (khums) was not reaching them. The relied-upon position is that Banu Hashim can never receive zakat, and a Hashimi cannot give zakat to another Hashimi.

Key Terms
بني هاشم (Banu Hashim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad, descendants of Hashim ibn 'Abd Manaf.
بني المطلب (Banu al-Muttalib): Descendants of al-Muttalib ibn 'Abd Manaf, a different clan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi, can a descendant of Banu Hashim receive zakat if he is poor?
A) Yes, if he is poor
B) No, never
C) Yes, if he is Muslim
D) Yes, if he is a scholar

Key Takeaway
Banu Hashim are permanently prohibited from receiving zakat; giving to them invalidates the zakat.

Mawali of Banu Hashim and Zakat Prohibition
Ruling on freed slaves of Banu Hashim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to the freed slave (mawla) of a Hashimi and explain the distinction from the mawla of a non-Hashimi.

Core Idea
The mawla of a Hashimi is treated like the Hashimi himself for zakat prohibition.

This short passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the matn of Nur al-Idah which includes the mawali (freed slaves) of Banu Hashim in the prohibition of receiving zakat. The commentator clarifies that this prohibition is restricted specifically to the mawla of a Hashimi, not to the mawla of a non-Hashimi rich person. Understanding this nuance prevents a common misunderstanding and ensures correct zakat distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act of giving zakat to the mawla of a Hashimi is ruled invalid (batil) in the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the condition
The prohibition is explicitly restricted to the mawla of a Hashimi; it does not apply to mawali of non-Hashimis.
The phrase 'قيد بمولى الهاشمي' indicates a qualification.
3
Understand the rationale
The mawla is associated with the Hashimi master, so the honor (and prohibition) extends to the freed slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و مواليهم: قيد بمولى الهاشمي؛ لأن مولى الغني يجوز الدفع إليه. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٦/٢]
And their mawali: it is restricted to the mawla of a Hashimi, because the mawla of a rich person may be given [zakat] to. [al-Bahr al-Raiq: 2/396]
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This brief passage clarifies that the mawali (freed slaves) of Banu Hashim are included in the prohibition. However, this is only for mawali of a Hashimi; mawali of a non-Hashimi rich person may receive zakat because they are considered separate individuals. The prohibition for mawali of Hashimi is due to their association with the honored clan.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla): A freed slave; here, a freed slave of a Hashimi person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A freed slave of a Hashimi is poor. Can he receive zakat?
A) Yes, because he is poor
B) No, because he is the mawla of a Hashimi
C) Yes, if he is Muslim
D) No, only if the Hashimi is rich

Key Takeaway
The mawla of a Hashimi is prohibited from receiving zakat due to association; this restriction does not apply to mawali of non-Hashimis.

Prohibition of Giving Zakat to Ascendants and Descendants
Explains the ruling on zakat to ascendants and descendants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to one's own ascendants and descendants, and identify which relatives are permissible recipients.

Core Idea
Zakat cannot be given to your own parents or children, but other relatives are encouraged.

This lesson examines a key rule in zakat distribution: the prohibition of giving zakat to one's own ascendants (parents, grandparents) and descendants (children, grandchildren). The underlying reason is that maintaining these relatives is already obligatory upon you, so zakat cannot replace that duty. However, giving zakat to other relatives—like siblings, uncles, and aunts—is not only permissible but also preferable as it combines charity with family ties. We will explore the scope of this prohibition and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and whether the ruling is valid/invalid. Here, giving zakat to one's own ascendants or descendants is ruled invalid (batil).
The ruling applies only to direct lineal relatives, not to in-laws or step-relatives.
2
Understand the reason for the prohibition
The prohibition is due to the obligatory maintenance (nafaqah) the payer owes to these relatives. Zakat, when given, would effectively fulfill that obligation, which is not allowed.
This reasoning is specific to the Hanafi school.
3
Define ascendants and descendants
Ascendants include parents, grandparents, and upward, no matter how high. Descendants include children, grandchildren, and downward, no matter how low.
The terms 'asl' and 'far'' are used in the text to denote these categories.
4
Identify exceptions and other relatives
Other blood relatives (e.g., siblings, uncles, aunts) are permissible and even preferred recipients. Also, a child born from zina (adultery) is not considered a legal descendant, so giving zakat to such a child is allowed.
The text clarifies that the prohibition is restricted to the payer's own lineage.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The operative ruling is that zakat cannot be given to one's own ascendants or descendants; all other relatives are valid recipients.
This is the standard Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل المزكي وفرعه، وزوجته ومملوكه ومكاتبه، ومعتق بعضه، وكفن ميت، وقضاء دينه وثمن قن يعتق ولو دفع بتحرِّ لمن ظنه مصرفا، فظهر بخلافه أجزأه، إلا أن يكون عبده ومكاتبه. وكره الإغناء.

وأصل المزكي إلخ أي لا يصح إلى أبيه وجده وإن علا، ولا إلى ولده وولد ولده وإن سفل، قيد بأصله وفرعه؛ لأن من سواهم من القرابة يجوز الدفع لهم، وهو أولى؛ لما فيه من الصلة الصدقة كالإخوة والأخوات والأعمام مع والعمات والأخوال والخالات الفقراء، وأطلق في فرعه فشمل ثابت النسب منه وغيره إذا كان مخلوقا من فلا يدفع إلى المخلوق من مائه بالزنا ولا إلى ولد أم ولده الذي نفاه [البحر الرائق بتصرف وزيادة: ٣٨٥/٢]
And the ascendant of the zakat-payer and his descendant, his wife, his owned slave, his mukatab, his part-free slave, the shroud of a dead person, and the repayment of his debt, and the price of a slave to be freed. And if he gives with ijtihad to someone he thinks is a recipient, but it turns out otherwise, it suffices, except if it is his slave or mukatab. And it is disliked to make [the recipient] rich.

'And the ascendant, etc.': i.e., it is not valid to give to his father or grandfather however high, nor to his child or grandchild however low. It was restricted to his ascendant and descendant because giving to other relatives is permissible and even preferable due to the connection of charity, such as poor brothers, sisters, paternal uncles and aunts, maternal uncles and aunts. And [the term] 'his descendant' was left general, so it includes those of established lineage and others, if the child is created from [his water], so he does not give to one created from his water through adultery, nor to the child of his umm walad whom he has disowned. [al-Bahr al-Raiq with adaptation and addition: 2/385]
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage states that zakat cannot be given to one's own ascendants (parents, grandparents) or descendants (children, grandchildren). The reason is that maintenance of these relatives is obligatory upon the payer, so giving zakat to them would effectively replace that obligation, which is not allowed. However, giving to other relatives (like siblings, uncles, aunts) is permissible and preferred because it combines charity with family ties. The commentary also clarifies that a child born from zina (adultery) is not considered a legal descendant, so giving zakat to such a child is permissible, while a child of a slave-concubine (umm walad) who has been disowned is also excluded from the prohibition.

Key Terms
أصل المزكي (asl al-muzakki): The ascendant of the zakat-payer, including parents and grandparents.
فرع المزكي (far' al-muzakki): The descendant of the zakat-payer, including children and grandchildren.
مملوك (mamluk): Owned slave; zakat cannot be given to one's own slave.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with the master to earn freedom; zakat cannot be given to one's own mukatab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man wants to give zakat to his poor grandfather. Is this valid?
A) Yes, because grandfather is a relative
B) No, because grandfather is an ascendant
C) Yes, if the grandfather is poor
D) No, only if the grandfather is not his legal heir

Key Takeaway
Giving zakat to one's own ascendants or descendants is invalid because it replaces obligatory maintenance; other relatives are permissible and recommended.

Zakat to Spouse – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling on zakat to spouse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on a wife giving zakat to her husband and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A wife may give zakat to her husband (Sahibayn), but a husband cannot give to his wife.

In this lesson, we examine a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of a wife giving zakat to her husband. The source text from Nur al-Idah clarifies that while a husband giving to his wife is invalid, the companions Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ from Imam Abu Hanifa on the reverse scenario. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between authoritative and non-authoritative positions within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the consensus on husband to wife
Identify the ruling that a man cannot give zakat to his wife, including a divorced woman in ‘iddah.
This is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that the author omits 'nor her husband' because there is a dispute. State the two positions: invalid (Abu Hanifa) vs valid (Sahibayn).
3
Analyse the proofs
The text does not provide explicit proofs; infer the rationale: husband benefits from wife's wealth vs husband is distinct recipient.
This step is inferential.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify the relied-upon position as that of the two companions, making it valid for a wife to give zakat to her husband.
The source text implies this by presenting the dispute with 'yes' as the second view.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a wife may discharge her zakat by giving to her poor husband; if she followed Abu Hanifa's view, she would need to give elsewhere.
This affects many families.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوجته: أي لا يجوز الدفع إلى زوجته. أطلق الزوجة فشمل الزوجة من وجه، فلا يجوز الدفع إلى معتدة من بائن ولو بثلاث [البحر الرائق: ٣٨٦/٢] و لم يقل وزوجها؛ لأن في دفع الزوجة إلى زوجها اختلافا، فلا يصح عند الإمام، ويصح عندهما
And his wife: i.e., it is not permissible to give to his wife. [The term] 'wife' was left general so it includes a wife from one aspect, so it is not permissible to give to a woman in ‘iddah of a final divorce even of three [divorces]. [al-Bahr al-Raiq: 2/386] And he did not say 'and her husband' because regarding giving by the wife to her husband there is disagreement; it is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and it is valid according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad].
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on giving zakat to one's spouse. For a man giving to his wife, it is invalid (according to all Hanafi imams) because the wife's maintenance is on the husband, so it is like giving to himself. The ruling includes even a divorced woman in her ‘iddah of a final divorce (bā’in). As for a wife giving to her husband, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa says it is invalid (because the husband is the wife's ascendant? Actually, the reason is that the husband benefits from his wife's wealth, similar to giving to oneself), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) say it is valid. The relied-upon position is that of the two companions, making it permissible for a wife to give zakat to her husband.

Key Terms
زوجة (zawjah): Wife; includes a wife in ‘iddah of a final divorce.
معتدة (mu'taddah): A woman in the waiting period after divorce; here, even a final divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, can a wife give her zakat to her poor husband?
A) Yes
B) No
C) Only if she is rich
D) Only with the husband's permission

Key Takeaway
The wife may give zakat to her husband according to the relied-upon Hanafi position; the husband may not give to his wife.

Zakat to One’s Own Slave and Mukatab: Ruling of Invalidity
Prohibition of giving zakat to owned slaves and partial slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to one's own slave, mukatab, and part-free slave, and explain why such payment is invalid.

Core Idea
Giving zakat to one's own slave or mukatab is invalid because it amounts to giving to oneself.

The text makes a clear ruling: paying zakat to certain categories of persons does not fulfill the obligation. Among them are those whose property ultimately belongs to the giver. Understanding this prevents a common mistake that could render one's zakat invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited recipients
List the three types: owned slave (mamlūk), slave under contract (mukātab), and part-free slave (mu‘taq ba‘ḍuh).
They share the characteristic of still being owned by the giver.
2
Understand the ruling term
The phrase 'lā yaṣiḥḥ' means the payment is invalid (bāṭil) – it does not fulfill the zakat obligation.
Bāṭil means the act is legally void; no reward is earned.
3
Grasp the underlying reason
Because the slave’s earnings and possessions belong to the master, giving to them is like transferring wealth from one hand to the other of the same person.
The ruling is based on the principle that one cannot discharge an obligation by paying oneself.
4
Apply to other cases
The ruling extends to any situation where the recipient’s property is owned by the giver, such as giving zakat to a company one wholly owns.
Modern analogies follow the same ‘illa (effective cause) of ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومملوكه أي لا يصح الدفع إلى هؤلاء.
And his owned slave: i.e., it is not valid to give to these [i.e., the listed individuals].
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This brief commentary confirms that the matn's prohibition includes giving zakat to one's own owned slave (mamlūk), mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), and part-free slave (mu‘taq ba‘ḍuhu). The reason is that giving to them is like giving to oneself, as their property ultimately belongs to the master. Therefore, such payment does not fulfill the zakat obligation.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamlūk): Owned slave, male or female.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has a contract to earn freedom by paying installments.
معتق بعضه (mu‘taq ba‘ḍuhu): A slave who has been partially freed; still partly owned by the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a person give his zakat to his own slave who is under a manumission contract (mukatab)?
A) Yes, because the slave is working for freedom
B) No, because the slave is still owned
C) Yes, if the slave is poor
D) No, only if the slave is a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
Paying zakat to one's own slave, mukatab, or part-free slave is invalid (batil) because the master retains ownership over them.

Zakat for the Shroud of the Deceased and the Permissible Trick
Ruling on using zakat for shroud and a permissible trick

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using zakat directly for a deceased person's shroud and explain the permissible trick (hilah) with its conditions.

Core Idea
Zakat cannot be given directly for a dead person's shroud; give it to a living poor person first, who then does the shrouding.

Zakat is an obligation that must reach a living recipient who can own wealth. But what if you wish to use your zakat for a pious purpose like shrouding a deceased relative? The Hanafi school provides a lawful stratagem (hilah) that fulfills the zakat obligation while achieving the goal. In this lesson, you will learn the ruling and the correct method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its direct ruling
Read the source: it states that paying zakat directly for shrouding a dead person is not valid (la yasihh). Understand that the act is the payment of zakat for the shroud.
The ruling is batil (invalid) when done directly.
2
Understand the underlying reason
The deceased cannot own property; zakat must be given to a living owner. Direct payment to a dead person's shroud is like giving to the deceased, which is invalid.
Zakat requires transfer of ownership to a living poor person.
3
Learn the permissible trick (hilah)
The trick is to give the zakat money as charity (sadaqah) to a living poor person, and then that poor person uses it to shroud the deceased. This way the zakat obligation is fulfilled and the deceased is shrouded.
The poor person must receive the money and then voluntarily spend it on the shroud.
4
Verify the conditions of the trick
The poor person must be a valid recipient of zakat (i.e., poor and Muslim). The intention (niyyah) of zakat must be made when giving to the poor person. The poor person must be alive at the time of receipt.
If the poor person is directed by the giver, it remains valid as long as the poor person owns the money.
5
Apply the same principle to other cases
The text extends the same trick to building mosques: give zakat to a poor person who then donates it to the mosque project. This shows the principle applies to any case where the direct recipient cannot own wealth (e.g., mosque, cemetery).
The trick ensures the zakat reaches a living owner before being used for the intended purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كفن :ميت أي لا يصح دفع الزكاة لتكفين ميت قال في "الدر" نقلا عن حيل "الأشباه": وحيلة التكفين بها التصدق على فقير ، ثم هو يكفن، فيكون الثواب لهما، وكذا في تعمير المساجد. [حاشية الطحطاوي بزيادة: ٧٢١]
And the shroud of a dead person: i.e., it is not valid to give zakat for shrouding a dead person. [The author] said in al-Durr, quoting from Hiyal al-Ashbah: 'And the trick for shrouding with it is to give charity to a poor person, then he shrouds [the dead], so the reward is for both. And similarly for building mosques.' [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi with addition: 721]
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage states that using zakat directly to purchase a shroud for a deceased person is invalid, because zakat must be given to a living recipient. The dead person cannot own wealth. However, a permissible trick (ḥīlah) is to give the zakat money to a poor person as charity, and then that poor person uses it to shroud the deceased. This way, the zakat obligation is fulfilled and the deceased is shrouded. The same trick applies to building mosques: give to a poor person who then donates to the mosque.

Key Terms
حيلة (hīlah): A lawful stratagem to achieve a permissible goal while complying with legal rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct way to use zakat for a deceased person's shroud?
A) Pay the shroud maker directly with zakat money.
B) Give the zakat money to a poor person, who then uses it to buy the shroud.
C) It is not allowed to use zakat for a shroud at all.
D) Give the zakat money to the mosque, and the mosque will arrange the shroud.

Key Takeaway
Zakat must be given to a living recipient; to benefit the deceased, give it to a poor person who then uses it for the shroud.

Using Zakat to Pay Debts: Conditions and Rulings
Ruling on using zakat to pay debts of deceased or living

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using zakat to pay a debt and identify the condition that makes it valid for a living debtor.

Core Idea
Zakat cannot pay a dead person's debt, but can pay a living debtor's debt only if he authorizes it.

This lesson examines a specific application of zakat: using zakat funds to repay someone's debt. The Hanafi school distinguishes between paying the debt of a deceased person and that of a living person, with the key condition being the debtor's authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
We are looking at the act of paying a debt using zakat funds. The first step is to determine whether this is generally permissible as a form of zakat disbursement.
2
Distinguish between deceased and living debtor
The passage makes a clear distinction: paying the debt of a deceased person is invalid, while paying the debt of a living person has further conditions.
3
Identify the condition for the living debtor
For a living debtor, the critical condition is that the debtor must authorize the payment. Without authorization, the payment is considered a voluntary gift, not zakat.
This condition ensures the debtor actually receives and owns the zakat before it is used to satisfy the debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضاء دينه قيد بقضاء دين الميت؛ لأنه لو قضى دين الحي إن قضاه بغير أمره يكون متبرعا، ولا يجزئه عن الزكاة، وإن قضاه بأمره جاز، ويكون القابض كالوكيل له في قبض الصدقة. [البحر الرائق: ٣٨٥/٢]
And the repayment of his debt: restricted to repaying the debt of the dead; because if he repays the debt of a living person without his order, he is a volunteer, and it does not suffice for his zakat. But if he repays it by his order, it is permissible, and the recipient is like his agent in receiving the charity. [al-Bahr al-Raiq: 2/385]
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on using zakat to pay debts. It is invalid to pay the debt of a deceased person directly, because the deceased cannot own the zakat. For a living person, if the zakat-payer repays the debt without the debtor's permission, it is considered a voluntary payment and does not fulfill the zakat obligation. However, if the debtor (who is eligible to receive zakat) instructs the payer to use the zakat to pay off his debt, then it is valid, as the debtor effectively receives the zakat and then repays his debt. In that case, the creditor is like an agent.

Key Terms
قضاء دين (qadā' dayn): Using zakat money to repay a debt owed by someone.
متبرع (mutabarri‘): A volunteer; one who gives voluntarily without obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a zakat-payer pay off his friend's debt without the friend's knowledge using zakat money?
A) Yes
B) No
C) Yes, if the friend is poor
D) No, only if the friend authorizes it

Key Takeaway
Zakat cannot be used to pay a deceased person's debt; for a living debtor, authorization is essential for validity.

Using Zakat to Buy and Free a Slave
Ruling statement: prohibition of using zakat funds to purchase a slave for emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using zakat to buy and free a slave and identify the correct method of discharging zakat.

Core Idea
Zakat must go directly to a poor free person; buying a slave with it fails.

This lesson addresses a common misconception: that using zakat to free a slave is a praiseworthy act and therefore valid. The Hanafi school clearly prohibits this because zakat must be given directly to an eligible recipient. We will explore the ruling, its rationale, and the correct alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act being ruled upon
The act is using zakat funds to purchase a slave with the intention of freeing him.
The slave is owned property, not a zakat recipient at the time of purchase.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is that this is not permissible (batil) and does not fulfill the zakat obligation.
The source text explicitly says 'it is not permissible'.
3
Explain the rationale
Zakat requires tamlik (transfer of ownership) to an eligible recipient (e.g., a poor free person). Buying a slave does not transfer ownership to a poor person; the slave becomes free but never received the zakat as a poor recipient.
The freed slave is not poor at the moment of the transaction; he was property.
4
Contrast with the correct method
The correct method is to give the zakat directly to a poor free person. That person can then use the money to buy and free a slave if they wish.
The intention of freeing a slave is achieved, but the zakat is properly discharged.
5
Apply to a scenario
If someone has $1000 zakat and wants to free a slave, they should give the $1000 to a poor person, who then may buy and free the slave.
This fulfills both the zakat obligation and the desire to free a slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثمن قن إلخ أي لا يجوز أن يشترى بها عبد فيعتق [تبيين الحقائق: ۱۲۱/۲]
And the price of a slave to be freed, etc.: i.e., it is not permissible to purchase a slave with it [zakat] and then free him. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 2/121]
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage states that zakat cannot be used to buy a slave for the purpose of freeing him. The reason is that the freed slave becomes the owner of his own person, but the zakat was not given directly to him as a recipient. The obligation of zakat is to transfer ownership to a qualified recipient (like a poor free person). Freeing a slave is a meritorious act but does not fulfill the zakat requirement. The correct way is to give the zakat to a poor person, who may then use it for any purpose.

Key Terms
قن (qinn): A male slave who is owned completely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on using zakat to buy a slave and free him?
A) Permissible
B) Not permissible and does not fulfill zakat
C) Permissible only if the slave is poor
D) Permissible only if the slave is Muslim

Key Takeaway
Zakat must be given directly to a qualified recipient; purchasing a slave to free him does not transfer ownership to a poor person and thus does not fulfill the zakat obligation.

Giving Zakat with Taharrī: When Mistake Does Not Invalidate
Ruling on giving zakat based on taharrī with intra-Hanafi disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat with taharrī and identify the three categories of payer's state.

Core Idea
Taharrī saves the zakat: genuine ijtihad makes it valid even if wrong.

Imagine giving your zakat to someone you truly believe is needy, only to discover he is wealthy. Does your zakat count? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer based on whether you made a genuine effort (taharrī) or not. This lesson examines the ruling and the three possible scenarios, along with an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is giving zakat based on assumption. The general ruling is that if the payer made taharrī and had dominant belief that the recipient was eligible, the zakat is valid even if later proven wrong.
This is the mufta bihi position of the Sahibayn.
2
Note the exception
The exception is if the recipient turns out to be the payer's own slave or mukatab. In that case, the zakat is not valid regardless of taharrī.
These recipients are inherently ineligible.
3
Examine the three categories per al-Zayla'i
Al-Zayla'i outlines three scenarios: (1) With taharrī and dominant belief – valid; (2) Without any thought – valid unless proven ineligible; (3) With doubt or without conviction – invalid unless proven eligible.
This categorization clarifies the ruling based on the payer's state.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagrees with the Sahibayn: if error is discovered, he says the zakat does not suffice. The Sahibayn hold that taharrī with dominant belief is sufficient.
This is a difference within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon opinion is the Sahibayn's view: with taharrī and dominant belief, it suffices even if mistaken.
This is the position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دفع إلخ: أي لو دفع المزكي الزكاة إلى رجل، وظن أنه يجوز دفع الزكاة إليه، أي ظن أنه فقير مثلا، ثم ظهر أنه لم يكن فقيرا، بل كان غنياً أجزأه، ولا يجب عليه أن يعيدها، إلا أن يظهر أن المدفوع إليه الزكاة كان عبد المزكي أو مكاتبه، وقال الزيلعي: وفي قوله: "دفع بتحر" إشارة إلى أنه إذا دفع بغير تحر وأخطأ لا يجزئه. فحاصله: أن هذه المسألة تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: الأول: أنه إذا تحرى وغلب على ظنه أنه مصرف، فهو جائز أصاب أو أخطأ عندهما ، خلافا لأبي يوسف الله فيما إذا تبين ،خطؤه، والثاني: أنه إذا دفعها ولم يخطر بباله أنه مصرف أم لا، فهو على الجواز إلّا إذا تبين أنه غير مصرف، والثالث: أنه إذا دفعها إليه، وهو شاك ولم يتحرّ، أو تحرى ولم يظهر له أنه مصرف، أو غلب على ظنه أنه ليس بمصرف فهو على الفساد إلا إذا تبين أنه مصرف. تبيين الحقائق بزيادة: ۱۲۹/۲]
And if he gives with ijtihad, etc.: i.e., if the zakat-payer gives zakat to a man, thinking that it is permissible to give zakat to him, i.e., thinking he is poor for example, then it appears that he was not poor but rich, it suffices him and he does not have to repeat it, except if it appears that the recipient was the zakat-payer's own slave or mukatab. And al-Zayla'i said: In his statement 'gave with ijtihad' there is an indication that if he gave without ijtihad and erred, it does not suffice him. The summary is that this issue is divided into three categories: First: that if he made ijtihad and it was his overwhelming belief that he is a recipient, it is valid whether he was correct or mistaken, according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], contrary to Abu Yusuf when his error becomes clear. Second: that if he gave it without its occurring to his mind whether he is a recipient or not, then it is upon permissibility unless it becomes clear that he is not a recipient. Third: that if he gave it to him while in doubt and did not make ijtihad, or made ijtihad but it did not become clear to him that he is a recipient, or his overwhelming belief was that he is not a recipient, then it is invalid unless it becomes clear that he is a recipient. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq with addition: 2/129]
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a zakat-payer gives to someone he thinks is eligible but later discovers otherwise. The general rule is that if he made a genuine effort (taharrī) and concluded the person was eligible, the zakat is valid even if a mistake is discovered, except when the recipient turns out to be his own slave or mukatab (those are inherently ineligible). Al-Zayla'i outlines three cases: (1) With taharrī and dominant belief: valid per Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf disagrees if error is discovered. (2) Without any thought: valid unless proven ineligible. (3) With doubt or without conviction: invalid unless proven eligible. The relied-upon position is that with taharrī and dominant belief, it suffices.

Key Terms
تحر (taharrī): Making a genuine effort to determine eligiblity of a zakat recipient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person gives zakat to someone he genuinely believes is poor, but later finds out he was rich. Is the zakat valid?
A) No, must repeat
B) Yes, if he made taharrī
C) No, unless the recipient was a relative
D) Yes, always

Key Takeaway
Zakat given with genuine effort (taharrī) and dominant belief of eligibility is valid even if a mistake in the recipient's status is discovered, except for one's own slave or mukatab.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Dislike of Making the Zakat Recipient Rich (Ighna')
Explains the dislike of making the recipient rich with two cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios in which giving zakat that makes the recipient rich is disliked (makruh) and explain the underlying principle.

Core Idea
Zakat should alleviate poverty, not create lasting wealth for the recipient.

This lesson unpacks the ruling of karaha (dislike) when zakat is given to a poor person in such a way that after meeting debts or dependents' needs, a complete nisab remains. Understanding this helps you apply zakat with proper balance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Recognise that the author states 'It is disliked to make [the recipient] rich.' This is a clear ruling of karaha (tanzihi).
Dislike means it is better to avoid, but the act is not sinful.
2
Understand the first scenario: debt
If the poor person has a debt, it is disliked to give him enough that after paying the debt, a full nisab remains surplus. Example: he owes 500 dirhams, you give 100—he still has nisab.
The nisab considered is after subtracting obligatory debts.
3
Understand the second scenario: dependents
If the poor person supports dependents, it is disliked to give him an amount that if divided among them (each getting less than nisab), he himself would have a full nisab left.
The focus is on surplus wealth, not temporary relief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره الإغناء إلخ : اعلم أن الإغناء المكروه على قسمين: الأول: أن يفضل عند الفقير نصاب كامل بعد قضاء دينه الواجب عليه مثلا كان عليه خمس مائة ، فأعطاه مائة درهم والثاني : إذا كان معيلا أي ذا عيال فيكره أن يعطيه مقدار ما لو وزعه على عياله ويعطي كل واحد منهم دون نصاب يفضل عنده نصاب كامل درهم الذهب أو الفضة وهذا هو الذي أشار إليه الماتن.
And it is disliked to make [the recipient] rich, etc.: Know that the disliked enrichment is of two types: First: that a complete nisab remains surplus with the poor person after paying his obligatory debt; for example, he owed five hundred [dirhams] and he gave him one hundred dirhams. Second: if he has dependents, i.e., a family, it is disliked to give him an amount that if distributed among his dependents and he gives each of them less than nisab, a complete nisab remains surplus with him, whether dirhams of gold or silver. And this is what the author (matn) indicated.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of karāha (disliked) regarding making the zakat recipient rich. It is makruh to give so much that after receiving the zakat, the recipient owns a full nisab as surplus over his needs. Two scenarios: (1) The recipient has a debt, and after paying it, he has a complete nisab left. For example, he owes 500 dirhams and is given 100 – still he has a nisab. (2) The recipient has dependents; it is makruh to give him an amount that if divided among his family would leave him with a nisab. The underlying principle is that zakat should alleviate poverty temporarily, not create independent wealth. The commentator notes this is what the original text (matn) referred to.

Key Terms
إغناء (ighnā'): Making the zakat recipient wealthy enough to own a nisab after receiving.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is it permissible to give zakat to a poor person in an amount that makes him own a full nisab after his debts?
A) Yes, permissible without dislike
B) Permissible but disliked
C) Forbidden
D) Permissible only if he has dependents

Key Takeaway
Giving zakat that leaves the recipient with a complete nisab after meeting needs is disliked, not forbidden.

Prohibited Recipients of Zakat and Ruling on Ijtihad
Lists prohibited recipients and ijtihad ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the individuals to whom zakat cannot be given according to the Hanafi school, and state the ruling when zakat is paid based on ijtihad but the recipient is later found to be ineligible.

Core Idea
Zakat cannot be given to ascendants, descendants, spouse, or slaves; but payment by ijtihad to an apparent eligible person is valid even if mistaken.

This lesson covers two distinct but related rulings: first, the categories of people who are prohibited from receiving zakat because they are already under the giver's maintenance obligation or ownership; second, the situation where a payer uses ijtihad (diligent effort) to identify an eligible recipient but turns out to be wrong. Understanding both is essential for correctly fulfilling the obligation of zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited recipients
List each category: the giver's ascendants (father, grandfather, etc.), descendants (children, grandchildren, etc.), wife, slaves, mukatib, partially freed slave, and also using zakat for shroud of a dead person, paying debts of the dead, or buying a slave to free.
The commentary also includes children born from adultery and denied children of umm walad.
2
Understand the rationale
These are prohibited because the giver is already obligated to support ascendants, descendants, and spouse, or because the recipient is owned by the giver (slaves) or the act does not transfer ownership to a poor person (shroud, debt payment without agency).
Other relatives like brothers, uncles, etc., are allowed and even preferred.
3
Analyze the ijtihad scenario
If the payer genuinely believes (through ijtihad) that a person is eligible (e.g., poor) and later it turns out false, the payment is valid and need not be repeated, unless the recipient was the payer's slave or mukatib.
If no ijtihad was made and the recipient is ineligible, the payment is invalid.
4
Distinguish the intra-madhhab differences
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a wife cannot give zakat to her husband, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it. Regarding ijtihad, Abu Yusuf differs when the error becomes clear.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that zakat to prohibited categories is invalid, and ijtihad payment is valid if genuinely practiced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[حاشية الطحطاوي بزيادة: ٧٢١] وقضاء دينه قيد بقضاء دين الميت؛ لأنه لو قضى دين الحي إن قضاه بغير أمره يكون متبرعا، ولا يجزئه عن الزكاة، وإن قضاه بأمره جاز، ويكون القابض كالوكيل له في قبض الصدقة. [البحر الرائق: ٣٨٥/٢] وثمن قن إلخ أي لا يجوز أن يشترى بها عبد فيعتق [تبيين الحقائق: ۱۲۱/۲] ولو دفع إلخ: أي لو دفع المزكي الزكاة إلى رجل، وظن أنه يجوز دفع الزكاة إليه، أي ظن أنه فقير مثلا، ثم ظهر أنه لم يكن فقيرا، بل كان غنياً أجزأه، ولا يجب عليه أن يعيدها، إلا أن يظهر أن المدفوع إليه الزكاة كان عبد المزكي أو مكاتبه، وقال الزيلعي: وفي قوله: "دفع بتحر" إشارة إلى أنه إذا دفع بغير تحر وأخطأ لا يجزئه. فحاصله: أن هذه المسألة تنقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: الأول: أنه إذا تحرى وغلب على ظنه أنه مصرف، فهو جائز أصاب أو أخطأ عندهما ، خلافا لأبي يوسف الله فيما إذا تبين ،خطؤه، والثاني: أنه إذا دفعها ولم يخطر بباله أنه مصرف أم لا، فهو على الجواز إلّا إذا تبين أنه غير مصرف، والثالث: أنه إذا دفعها إليه، وهو شاك ولم يتحرّ، أو تحرى ولم يظهر له أنه مصرف، أو غلب على ظنه أنه ليس بمصرف فهو على الفساد إلا إذا تبين أنه مصرف. تبيين الحقائق بزيادة: ۱۲۹/۲] بخلافه أي ظهر أنه لم يكن مصرفا للزكاة.

مصرف الزكاة
يفضل للفقير نصاب بعد قضاء دينه وبعد إعطاء كل فرد من عياله دون
نصاب من المدفوع إليه، وإلا فلا يكره. وندب إغناؤه عن السؤال. وكره نقلها بعد تمام الحول لبلد آخر لغير قريب وأحوج وأورع وأنفع للمسلمين بتعليم، والأفضل ولو ما دون مسافة القصر صرفها للأقرب فالأقرب من كل ذي رحم محرم منه، ثم لجيرانه، ثم لأهل محلّته، ثم لأهل ،حرفته ثم لأهل بلدته وقال الشيخ أبو حفص الكبير له: لا تقبل صدقة
الرجل وقرابته محاويج، حتى يبدأ بهم، فيسد حاجتهم.
جمع محوج أي مفتقر
بعد قضاء إلخ ولو دفع مائتي دراهم فأكثر لمديون لا يفضل له بعد دينه نصاب لا يكره. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٥/٢] من عياله: لو كان معيلا إذا وزع المأخوذ على عياله ولم يصب كلا منهم نصاب لا يكره. [البحر الرائق بتصرف: ٣٩٥/٢] لبلد آخر المعتبر في الزكاة مكان المال حتى لو كان هو في بلد وماله في بلد أخرى يفرق في
موضع المال، وفي صدقة الفطر يعتبر مكانه لا مكان أولاده الصغار وعبيده في الصحيح. [تبيين الحقائق: ١٣١/٢] لغير قريب فإن نقلها إلى قرابته أو إلى قوم هم إليها أحوج من أهل بلده لا يكره، قالوا: الأفضل في صرف الصدقة أن يصرفها إلى إخوته، ثم أولادهم، ثم أعمامه الفقراء، ثم أخواله الفقراء، ثم ذوي الأرحام، ثم جيرانه، ثم أهل سكنه، ثم أهل مصره تبيين الحقائق بتصرف: ۱۳۱/۲] وأورع لو نقلها إلى فقير في بلد آخر أورع وأصلح كما فعل معاذ لا يكره، ولهذا قيل: التصدق على العالم الفقير أفضل البحر الرائق بتصرف: ٣٩٥/٢]
The Book of Zakat. The Recipients of Zakat. And the origin of the giver and his offspring, and his wife, and his slave, and his mukatib, and one partly freed, and the shroud of a dead person, and the payment of his debt, and the price of a slave to be freed, and if he pays with ijtihad to one he thinks is a recipient, and it turns out otherwise, it suffices him, unless it is his slave or mukatib. And it is disliked to make (the recipient) rich. ... And his origin and his offspring etc. means it is not valid to pay to his father and grandfather however high, nor to his child and grandchild however low. He restricted by his origin and offspring; because other relatives may be paid to, and it is preferable due to the connection of charity, like brothers, sisters, uncles, aunts, and maternal uncles and aunts if they are poor. And he made it absolute in his offspring, so it includes one of established lineage and others if created from his semen: so he does not pay to one created from his semen by adultery, nor to the child of his umm walad that he denied. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:385 with modification and addition] And his wife: i.e., it is not permissible to pay to his wife. He said wife absolute, so it includes a wife in some sense: it is not permissible to pay to a woman in a ba'in divorce even if by three. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:386] And he did not say 'her husband'; because paying a wife to her husband is disputed: it is not valid according to Imam, and valid according to the two. And his slave: i.e., it is not valid to pay to these. And the shroud of a dead person: i.e., it is not valid to pay zakat to shroud a dead person. He said in al-Durr quoting from Hiyal al-Ashbah: The trick for shrouding him is to give in charity to a poor person, then he shrouds him, so the reward is for both, and similarly for building mosques. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi with addition p. 721] And payment of his debt: restricted to payment of the dead person's debt; because if he pays the debt of a living person without his order, he is a volunteer, and it does not count for zakat, but if he pays with his order, it is permissible, and the receiver is like his agent in receiving the charity. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:385] And the price of a slave etc.: i.e., it is not permissible to buy a slave with it and free him. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:121] And if he pays etc.: i.e., if the payer of zakat pays zakat to a man, thinking that it is permissible to pay to him, i.e. thinking he is poor etc., then it becomes clear that he was not poor but rich, it suffices him, and he is not required to repeat it, unless it becomes clear that the recipient was the payer's slave or mukatib. And al-Zayla'i said: In his statement 'pays with ijtihad' there is an indication that if he pays without ijtihad and errs, it does not suffice him. So the summary: this issue divides into three divisions: First: if he exercises ijtihad and it is his overwhelming opinion that he is a recipient, it is valid whether he is correct or mistaken according to the two, contrary to Abu Yusuf when his error becomes clear. Second: if he pays and it does not occur to him whether he is a recipient or not, it is valid unless it becomes clear that he is not a recipient. Third: if he pays while doubting and not exercising ijtihad, or he exercises ijtihad but it does not become clear to him that he is a recipient, or his overwhelming opinion is that he is not a recipient, then it is invalid unless it becomes clear that he is a recipient. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:129 with addition] Contrary to it: i.e., it becomes clear that he was not a recipient of zakat. And it is disliked to make him rich etc.: Know that making rich that is disliked is of two types: First: that a full nisab remains with the poor after paying his obligatory debt, e.g., he owed five hundred, so he gave him one hundred dirhams. Second: if he has dependents, i.e., a family, it is disliked to give him an amount that if he distributed it among his dependents, each would get less than nisab, but a full nisab remains with him. This is what the matn author referred to. That a full nisab remains for the poor after paying his debt and after giving each of his dependents less than nisab from what was given to him, otherwise it is not disliked. And it is recommended to make him need-free from asking. And it is disliked to transfer zakat after the completion of the year to another city except for a close relative, or more needy, or more pious, or more beneficial to Muslims through teaching, and the best, even if less than travel distance, is to give it to the closest then closest of every mahram relative, then to his neighbors, then to the people of his quarter, then to the people of his trade, then to the people of his city. And Shaykh Abu Hafs al-Kabir said to him: Do not accept the charity of a man while his relatives are in need, until he begins with them and fulfills their need. (Mahawij: plural of muhtaj meaning needy). After paying etc.: even if he pays two hundred dirhams or more to a debtor such that no nisab remains after his debt, it is not disliked. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:395] From his dependents: if he has dependents and when he distributes the amount taken among them, none of them gets nisab, it is not disliked. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:395 with modification] To another city: what matters in zakat is the place of wealth, so if he is in one city and his wealth is in another, he distributes it where the wealth is. And in sadaqat al-fitr, his own place is considered, not the place of his young children and slaves, according to the correct view. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:131] Except for a close relative: if he transfers it to his relatives or to people who are more in need than his own townspeople, it is not disliked. They said: The best in giving charity is to give it to his brothers, then their children, then his poor uncles, then his poor maternal uncles, then dhawi al-arham, then his neighbors, then the people of his residence, then the people of his city. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:131 with modification] And more pious: if he transfers it to a poor person in another city who is more pious and righteous, as Mu'adh did, it is not disliked. For this reason it was said: Giving charity to a poor scholar is better. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:395 with modification]
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah explains the rules governing the distribution of zakat, specifically who may and may not receive it. The text is a commentary (sharh) on the original matn. It begins by listing those to whom zakat cannot be given: the giver's own parents and grandparents (even ascending), his children and grandchildren (even descending), his wife, his slaves and mukatib (a slave under contract for freedom), a partly freed slave, also paying for a dead person's shroud, paying off the deceased's debts, and buying a slave to free him. The commentary explains each: zakat cannot fulfill obligations to those the giver is already obliged to support, nor can it be used for expenses that are not the direct ownership transfer to a poor person.

The text then discusses a scenario where a payer gives zakat to someone whom he, after making ijtihad (diligent effort), believes to be eligible (e.g., poor), but later discovers the person was actually wealthy. According to the Hanafi school (except Abu Yusuf in one case), the payment is valid and does not need to be repeated, provided the payer acted on a genuine ijtihad. If he paid without any ijtihad or doubtfully, it is invalid unless the recipient turns out to be eligible. The exception is if the recipient turns out to be the payer's own slave or mukatib, in which case the zakat is not valid.

The next ruling covers the dislike (karahiyyah) of making a recipient rich, i.e., giving him so much that after meeting his debts and dependent needs, he ends up with a full nisab (minimum threshold for zakat). There are two scenarios: giving a debtor enough to leave him a full nisab after debt, or giving a family man an amount that, if distributed to his dependents, leaves him with a full nisab. It is not disliked if no nisab remains.

Finally, the text addresses transferring zakat to another city after the year has passed. It is generally disliked unless the transfer is to a close relative, or the recipients are more needy, or the transfer results in greater benefit (e.g., to a poor scholar). The preferred order of giving is: to one's own poor mahram relatives (such as brothers, uncles, etc.), then neighbors, then residents of one's quarter, then people of one's trade, then people of one's city. The principle is to start with those who are closest in relationship and need.

Key Terms
أصل المزكي (asl al-muzakki): The ascendants of the zakat giver, i.e., father, grandfather, and above, regardless of how high.
فرعه (far'uhu): The descendants of the zakat giver, i.e., children, grandchildren, etc., regardless of how low.
مصرف (masraf): A category of eligible recipients of zakat as defined by Shariah.
تحر (taharri): Diligent effort or ijtihad to determine the correct recipient of zakat when it is uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, whom among the following can you NOT give your zakat to?
A) Your poor brother
B) Your poor uncle (paternal)
C) Your poor son
D) Your poor neighbor

Key Takeaway
Zakat must be given to eligible recipients; giving it to ascendants, descendants, spouse, or slaves invalidates the payment, but genuine ijtihad can salvage a mistaken payment.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Etiquette of Zakat Distribution: Avoiding Ighna' and Priority Order
Rules on ighna' and priority order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on making a zakat recipient rich (ighna'), identify when it is disliked, and state the preferred order for distributing zakat among relatives and others.

Core Idea
It is disliked to give zakat to a poor person such that he becomes rich; the best distribution prioritizes needy relatives, then neighbors, then local community.

After determining eligible recipients, the payer should consider how much to give and to whom to give priority. This lesson covers two important guidelines: first, the dislike (karahiyyah) of giving so much that the recipient ends up with a full nisab after immediate needs; second, the recommended order of preference, starting with one's own needy mahram relatives, then neighbors, then people of one's locality. These rules ensure that zakat fulfills its social purpose without creating dependency.

Guided Path
1
Define ighna'
Ighna' means giving the recipient so much that after paying his debts and meeting the needs of his dependents, a full nisab (minimum zakatable amount) remains with him.
The nisab is the threshold for zakat liability.
2
Identify when ighna' is disliked
It is disliked if the recipient ends up with a nisab surplus. Two examples: (1) a debtor who after paying debt has a nisab left; (2) a person with dependents who after distributing the zakat among them each gets less than nisab but he himself has a nisab left.
If no nisab remains, there is no dislike.
3
State the recommended priority order
The best order is: (1) one's own poor mahram relatives (brothers, sisters, uncles, etc.), (2) neighbors, (3) people of one's quarter, (4) people of one's trade, (5) people of one's city. Starting with the closest is preferred.
Transferring zakat to another city is generally disliked, but exceptions exist for relatives, greater need, or greater benefit (e.g., to a poor scholar).
4
Understand the exception for transfer
Transferring zakat to another city is not disliked if done for a close relative, or to people more needy, or if the recipient is more pious or beneficial for Muslims (e.g., a scholar).
The place of wealth is considered: if the giver is in one city and his wealth in another, the zakat is distributed where the wealth is located.

نصاب من المدفوع إليه، وإلا فلا يكره. وندب إغناؤه عن السؤال. وكره نقلها بعد تمام الحول لبلد آخر لغير قريب وأحوج وأورع وأنفع للمسلمين بتعليم، والأفضل ولو ما دون مسافة القصر صرفها للأقرب فالأقرب من كل ذي رحم محرم منه، ثم لجيرانه، ثم لأهل محلّته، ثم لأهل ،حرفته ثم لأهل بلدته وقال الشيخ أبو حفص الكبير له: لا تقبل صدقة
