جمع محوج أي مفتقر
بعد قضاء إلخ ولو دفع مائتي دراهم فأكثر لمديون لا يفضل له بعد دينه نصاب لا يكره. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٥/٢] من عياله: لو كان معيلا إذا وزع المأخوذ على عياله ولم يصب كلا منهم نصاب لا يكره. [البحر الرائق بتصرف: ٣٩٥/٢] لبلد آخر المعتبر في الزكاة مكان المال حتى لو كان هو في بلد وماله في بلد أخرى يفرق في

[تبيين الحقائق: ١٣١/٢] لغير قريب فإن نقلها إلى قرابته أو إلى قوم هم إليها أحوج من أهل بلده لا يكره، قالوا: الأفضل في صرف الصدقة أن يصرفها إلى إخوته، ثم أولادهم، ثم أعمامه الفقراء، ثم أخواله الفقراء، ثم ذوي الأرحام، ثم جيرانه، ثم أهل سكنه، ثم أهل مصره تبيين الحقائق بتصرف: ۱۳۱/۲] وأورع لو نقلها إلى فقير في بلد آخر أورع وأصلح كما فعل معاذ لا يكره، ولهذا قيل: التصدق على العالم الفقير أفضل البحر الرائق بتصرف: ٣٩٥/٢]
The Book of Zakat. The Recipients of Zakat. And the origin of the giver and his offspring, and his wife, and his slave, and his mukatib, and one partly freed, and the shroud of a dead person, and the payment of his debt, and the price of a slave to be freed, and if he pays with ijtihad to one he thinks is a recipient, and it turns out otherwise, it suffices him, unless it is his slave or mukatib. And it is disliked to make (the recipient) rich. ... And his origin and his offspring etc. means it is not valid to pay to his father and grandfather however high, nor to his child and grandchild however low. He restricted by his origin and offspring; because other relatives may be paid to, and it is preferable due to the connection of charity, like brothers, sisters, uncles, aunts, and maternal uncles and aunts if they are poor. And he made it absolute in his offspring, so it includes one of established lineage and others if created from his semen: so he does not pay to one created from his semen by adultery, nor to the child of his umm walad that he denied. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:385 with modification and addition] And his wife: i.e., it is not permissible to pay to his wife. He said wife absolute, so it includes a wife in some sense: it is not permissible to pay to a woman in a ba'in divorce even if by three. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:386] And he did not say 'her husband'; because paying a wife to her husband is disputed: it is not valid according to Imam, and valid according to the two. And his slave: i.e., it is not valid to pay to these. And the shroud of a dead person: i.e., it is not valid to pay zakat to shroud a dead person. He said in al-Durr quoting from Hiyal al-Ashbah: The trick for shrouding him is to give in charity to a poor person, then he shrouds him, so the reward is for both, and similarly for building mosques. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi with addition p. 721] And payment of his debt: restricted to payment of the dead person's debt; because if he pays the debt of a living person without his order, he is a volunteer, and it does not count for zakat, but if he pays with his order, it is permissible, and the receiver is like his agent in receiving the charity. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:385] And the price of a slave etc.: i.e., it is not permissible to buy a slave with it and free him. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:121] And if he pays etc.: i.e., if the payer of zakat pays zakat to a man, thinking that it is permissible to pay to him, i.e. thinking he is poor etc., then it becomes clear that he was not poor but rich, it suffices him, and he is not required to repeat it, unless it becomes clear that the recipient was the payer's slave or mukatib. And al-Zayla'i said: In his statement 'pays with ijtihad' there is an indication that if he pays without ijtihad and errs, it does not suffice him. So the summary: this issue divides into three divisions: First: if he exercises ijtihad and it is his overwhelming opinion that he is a recipient, it is valid whether he is correct or mistaken according to the two, contrary to Abu Yusuf when his error becomes clear. Second: if he pays and it does not occur to him whether he is a recipient or not, it is valid unless it becomes clear that he is not a recipient. Third: if he pays while doubting and not exercising ijtihad, or he exercises ijtihad but it does not become clear to him that he is a recipient, or his overwhelming opinion is that he is not a recipient, then it is invalid unless it becomes clear that he is a recipient. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:129 with addition] Contrary to it: i.e., it becomes clear that he was not a recipient of zakat. And it is disliked to make him rich etc.: Know that making rich that is disliked is of two types: First: that a full nisab remains with the poor after paying his obligatory debt, e.g., he owed five hundred, so he gave him one hundred dirhams. Second: if he has dependents, i.e., a family, it is disliked to give him an amount that if he distributed it among his dependents, each would get less than nisab, but a full nisab remains with him. This is what the matn author referred to. That a full nisab remains for the poor after paying his debt and after giving each of his dependents less than nisab from what was given to him, otherwise it is not disliked. And it is recommended to make him need-free from asking. And it is disliked to transfer zakat after the completion of the year to another city except for a close relative, or more needy, or more pious, or more beneficial to Muslims through teaching, and the best, even if less than travel distance, is to give it to the closest then closest of every mahram relative, then to his neighbors, then to the people of his quarter, then to the people of his trade, then to the people of his city. And Shaykh Abu Hafs al-Kabir said to him: Do not accept the charity of a man while his relatives are in need, until he begins with them and fulfills their need. (Mahawij: plural of muhtaj meaning needy). After paying etc.: even if he pays two hundred dirhams or more to a debtor such that no nisab remains after his debt, it is not disliked. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:395] From his dependents: if he has dependents and when he distributes the amount taken among them, none of them gets nisab, it is not disliked. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:395 with modification] To another city: what matters in zakat is the place of wealth, so if he is in one city and his wealth is in another, he distributes it where the wealth is. And in sadaqat al-fitr, his own place is considered, not the place of his young children and slaves, according to the correct view. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:131] Except for a close relative: if he transfers it to his relatives or to people who are more in need than his own townspeople, it is not disliked. They said: The best in giving charity is to give it to his brothers, then their children, then his poor uncles, then his poor maternal uncles, then dhawi al-arham, then his neighbors, then the people of his residence, then the people of his city. [Tibyan al-Haqa'iq 2:131 with modification] And more pious: if he transfers it to a poor person in another city who is more pious and righteous, as Mu'adh did, it is not disliked. For this reason it was said: Giving charity to a poor scholar is better. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:395 with modification]
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah explains the rules governing the distribution of zakat, specifically who may and may not receive it. The text is a commentary (sharh) on the original matn. It begins by listing those to whom zakat cannot be given: the giver's own parents and grandparents (even ascending), his children and grandchildren (even descending), his wife, his slaves and mukatib (a slave under contract for freedom), a partly freed slave, also paying for a dead person's shroud, paying off the deceased's debts, and buying a slave to free him. The commentary explains each: zakat cannot fulfill obligations to those the giver is already obliged to support, nor can it be used for expenses that are not the direct ownership transfer to a poor person.

The text then discusses a scenario where a payer gives zakat to someone whom he, after making ijtihad (diligent effort), believes to be eligible (e.g., poor), but later discovers the person was actually wealthy. If he paid without any ijtihad or doubtfully, it is invalid unless the recipient turns out to be eligible. The exception is if the recipient turns out to be the payer's own slave or mukatib, in which case the zakat is not valid.

The next ruling covers the dislike (karahiyyah) of making a recipient rich, i.e., giving him so much that after meeting his debts and dependent needs, he ends up with a full nisab (minimum threshold for zakat). It is not disliked if no nisab remains.

The preferred order of giving is: to one's own poor mahram relatives (such as brothers, uncles, etc.), then neighbors, then residents of one's quarter, then people of one's trade, then people of one's city. The principle is to start with those who are closest in relationship and need.

Key Terms
إغناء (ighna'): To make the recipient rich, i.e., give him so much that he becomes owner of a full nisab after meeting his basic needs and debts.
تحر (taharri): Diligent effort or ijtihad to determine the correct recipient of zakat when it is uncertain.

A) A poor neighbor
B) A poor brother
C) A poor scholar in another city

Key Takeaway
When distributing zakat, avoid giving so much that the recipient becomes rich (disliked), and prioritize needy relatives, then neighbors, then local community.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate

Preferential Distribution of Zakat: Priority and Dislike of Transfer
Rulings on to whom zakat should be given and disliked transfers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended order of zakat recipients, identify when transferring zakat is disliked, and apply the ruling to a scenario of zakat distribution.

Core Idea
Give zakat to relatives and locals first; transferring without need is disliked.

This passage from Nur al-Idah provides guidance on the optimal distribution of zakat. It clarifies that while zakat can be given to any eligible recipient, the Sharia recommends giving priority to one's relatives and local community. Transferring zakat to another town without a valid reason is discouraged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key rulings
The passage states: it is preferred (yufaddal) to leave the poor with a nisab after debts and family needs; it is recommended (yundabu) to make him self-sufficient; it is disliked (yukrahu) to transfer zakat after the year to another town without a valid reason; and the best practice is to give to relatives in order.
These are not obligatory but recommended and disliked rulings.
2
Understand the condition for the preference
The preference for leaving the poor with a nisab applies if after paying debts and giving each family member less than a nisab, the recipient still has something. If not, it is not disliked.
This ensures the recipient does not become ineligible for zakat.
3
Define the dislike of transfer
Transferring zakat to another town after the year is disliked (makruh tanzih) unless done for a relative, a more needy person, a more pious person, or someone who benefits Muslims through teaching.
The dislike is due to the local poor having priority.
4
Specify the order of recipients
The best order is: nearest mahram relative, then neighbors, then people of the same district, then same profession, then same town. The commentary adds an order for charity: brothers, their children, paternal uncles, maternal uncles, relatives by marriage, neighbors, residents, then fellow citizens.
This order is recommended for optimal reward.
5
Apply the rulings to a scenario
Consider a person with surplus zakat. He should first check his needy relatives, then local poor, and only transfer to a far town if the recipient is more needy or pious.
This reflects the principle of beginning with the nearest.

يفضل للفقير نصاب بعد قضاء دينه وبعد إعطاء كل فرد من عياله دون نصاب من المدفوع إليه، وإلا فلا يكره. وندب إغناؤه عن السؤال. وكره نقلها بعد تمام الحول لبلد آخر لغير قريب وأحوج وأورع وأنفع للمسلمين بتعليم، والأفضل ولو ما دون مسافة القصر صرفها للأقرب فالأقرب من كل ذي رحم محرم منه، ثم لجيرانه، ثم لأهل محلّته، ثم لأهل حرفته ثم لأهل بلدته وقال الشيخ أبو حفص الكبير له: لا تقبل صدقة الرجل وقرابته محاويج، حتى يبدأ بهم، فيسد حاجتهم.
جمع محوج أي مفتقر
بعد قضاء إلخ ولو دفع مائتي دراهم فأكثر لمديون لا يفضل له بعد دينه نصاب لا يكره. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٥/٢] من عياله: لو كان معيلا إذا وزع المأخوذ على عياله ولم يصب كلا منهم نصاب لا يكره. [البحر الرائع بتصرف: ٣٩٥/٢] لبلد آخر المعتبر في الزكاة مكان المال حتى لو كان هو في بلد وماله في بلد أخرى يفرق في موضع المال، وفي صدقة الفطر يعتبر مكانه لا مكان أولاده الصغار وعبيده في الصحيح. [تبيين الحقائق: ١٣١/٢] لغير قريب فإن نقلها إلى قرابته أو إلى قوم هم إليها أحوج من أهل بلده لا يكره، قالوا: الأفضل في صرف الصدقة أن يصرفها إلى إخوته، ثم أولادهم، ثم أعمامه الفقراء، ثم أخواله الفقراء، ثم ذوي الأرحام، ثم جيرانه، ثم أهل سكنه، ثم أهل مصره [تبيين الحقائق بتصرف: ۱۳۱/۲] وأورع لو نقلها إلى فقير في بلد آخر أورع وأصلح كما فعل معاذ لا يكره، ولهذا قيل: التصدق على العالم الفقير أفضل [البحر الرائق بتصرف: ٣٩٥/٢]
Disposal of Zakat. It is preferred that the poor person be left with a nisab after paying his debt and after giving each member of his family less than a nisab from the recipient, otherwise it is not disliked. It is recommended to make him self-sufficient so he does not ask. It is disliked to transfer it after the completion of the year to another town for other than a relative, more needy, more pious, or more beneficial for Muslims through teaching. The best, even if less than the distance of qasr, is to give it to the nearest then the nearest among every relative with whom marriage is prohibited, then to his neighbors, then to the people of his district, then to the people of his profession, then to the people of his town. And the great Shaykh Abu Hafs said to him: A man's charity is not accepted while his relatives are needy until he begins with them and fulfills their need. (Mahawij is plural of mahwaj, meaning needy). After paying etc.: Even if one gives two hundred dirhams or more to a debtor such that nothing remains after his debt up to a nisab, it is not disliked. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/395] From his family: If he is a breadwinner, when he distributes what he takes to his family and none of them gets a nisab, it is not disliked. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification 2/395] To another town: The consideration in zakat is the location of the wealth, so even if he is in one town and his wealth is in another town, he distributes in the place of the wealth. In sadaqat al-fitr, his location is considered, not the location of his minor children and slaves, according to the correct view. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 2/131] For other than a relative: If he transfers it to his relatives or to people who are more needy than the people of his town, it is not disliked. They said: The best in giving charity is to give it to his brothers, then their children, then his poor paternal uncles, then his poor maternal uncles, then relatives by marriage, then his neighbors, then the people of his residence, then the people of his city. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq with modification 2/131] And more pious: If he transfers it to a poor person in another town who is more pious and righteous, as Mu'adh did, it is not disliked. For this reason it is said: Giving charity to a poor scholar is better. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification 2/395]
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage, from the section on the disposal of zakat, provides rulings on how and to whom zakat should be distributed. It begins by stating a preference: when giving zakat to a poor person, it is recommended to leave him with a nisab (minimum wealth threshold) after paying his debts and after giving each of his family members less than a nisab (so they remain eligible for zakat). Otherwise, if this condition is not met, it is not disliked. It is recommended to give enough so that the recipient does not need to ask for charity. The passage then discusses transferring zakat to another town after the year has completed: it is disliked to do so for no valid reason, such as taking it to a relative, to someone more needy, to someone more pious, or to someone who can benefit Muslims through teaching. The best practice, even if the destination is within the distance of qasr (short travel), is to give to relatives in order of closeness: first to those with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (mahram), then neighbors, then people of the same district, then those of the same profession, then townspeople. Shaykh Abu Hafs al-Kabir is quoted: a man's charity is not accepted while his relatives are needy until he begins with them. The commentary explains that if one gives two hundred dirhams or more to a debtor such that after paying his debt nothing remains to reach a nisab, it is not disliked. Similarly, if a breadwinner distributes zakat among his family members and none receives a full nisab, it is not disliked. Regarding transfer, the rule in zakat is that the location of the wealth matters, not the giver's location. In sadaqat al-fitr, the giver's location is considered. Transferring to relatives or more needy people is not disliked. The preferred order for giving charity is brothers, their children, poor paternal uncles, poor maternal uncles, other relatives, neighbors, residents of the same area, then fellow citizens. Transferring to a pious person in another town is not disliked, as exemplified by Mu'adh. Giving to a poor scholar is considered better.

Key Terms
مصرف الزكاة (masrif al-zakat): The category of recipients and distribution rules for zakat
محاويج (mahawij): Plural of mahwaj, meaning needy people

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on transferring zakat to another town after the year has completed, if the recipients there are not relatives or more needy?
A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Recommended (mustahabb)
C) Disliked (makruh)
D) Permissible (mubah)

Key Takeaway
The priority in zakat distribution is to begin with the nearest relatives and local poor; transferring without cause is disliked.

Zakat — Almsgiving
beginner
8 min
Conditions for Obligation of Zakat al-Fitr and Payment for Dependents
Opening ruling on obligation conditions and dependents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that make Zakat al-Fitr obligatory and identify whom a person must pay for.

Core Idea
Zakat al-Fitr is wajib at dawn of Eid upon every free Muslim owning nisab, covering self and minor poor children.

This passage from Nur al-Idah opens the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr by laying out the essential conditions for obligation: freedom, Islam, ownership of nisab free from debt and basic needs, and the specific time of dawn of Eid. It then clarifies that the obligation covers oneself and one's minor poor children. If the children are wealthy, they must pay from their own wealth. Understanding these conditions ensures correct observance of this annual duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its level
The text begins 'tajibu' (it is obligatory). Recognize that Zakat al-Fitr is wajib, not fard, in the Hanafi school because it is established by the Sunnah with a zanni thubut. Note the ruling level for the lesson.
The source explicitly says 'tajibu' – obligatory.
2
Extract the conditions for the payer
From the opening line, list the conditions: free (not a slave), Muslim, owner of a nisab (minimum wealth) that is not for trade, free from debt and basic needs (dwelling, furnishings, etc.), and the time must be dawn of Eid al-Fitr.
Nisab here means sufficiency beyond essentials – no growth or year required.
3
Determine whom the obligation covers
The text says 'for himself and his minor poor children'. Understand that the obligation is for the payer himself and for dependents for whom he has full maintenance and guardianship – specifically minor children who are poor. If a child is wealthy, the father pays from the child's wealth.
The phrase 'his minor poor children' is restrictive: foreign minors are excluded, and wealthy minors pay from their own property.
4
Note the time trigger and exceptions
The obligation is linked to dawn of Eid. If a person dies before dawn, the obligation lapses. If a child is born after dawn, no obligation for that child. Similarly, a person who accepts Islam after dawn is not obligated for that year.
Time is a condition for both obligation and payment – see the commentary quoting Tabyin al-Haqa'iq.

Source Text

باب صدقة الفطر
tجب على حر مسلم مالك لنصاب أو قيمته وإن لم يحل عليه الحول، عند طلوع فجر يوم الفطر، ولم يكن للتجارة، فارغ عن الدين وحاجته الأصلية وحوائج عياله، والمعتبر أي وإن لم يكن نعت نصاب في فيها الكفاية لا التقدير، وهي مسكنه وأثاثه وثيابه وفرسه وسلاحه وعبيده للخدمة، هو متاع البيت فيخرجها عن نفسه وأولاده الصغار الفقراء، وإن كانوا أغنياء يخرجها من مالهم، ولا تجب على الجد في ظاهر الرواية، واختير أن الجد كالأب عند فقده أو فقره الأب
على حر مسلم إلخ شرط الحرية، ليتحقق التمليك، فلا تجب على العبد، والإسلام؛ لتقع قربة، فلا تجب على الكافر، وملك النصاب؛ لأنها وجبت لإغناء الفقير، والإغناء من غير الغني لا يكون. اعلم أن النصب ثلاثة: نصاب يشترط فيه النماء، وتتعلق به الزكاة وسائر الأحكام المتعلقة بالمال النامي. ونصاب تجب به أحكام أربعة حرمة الصدقة، ووجوب الأضحية، وصدقة الفطر، ونفقة الأقارب، ولا يشترط فيه النمو بالتجارة ولا حولان الحول ونصاب تثبت به حرمة السؤال، وهو ما إذا كان عنده قوت يومه عند بعض، وقال بعضهم : هو أن يملك خمسين درهما. [حاشية الطحطاوي بزيادة: ٧٢٣] عند طلوع إلخ بيان لوقت وجوب أدائها، وهو منصوب وعلى أنه ظرف؛ لـ "تجب" أول الباب، فمن مات قبل طلوع الفجر أو ولد أو أسلم بعده لا تجب عليه [تبيين الحقائق: ١٤١/٢] عن نفسه: شروع في بيان السبب، وهو رأسه، وما كان في معناه ممن يمونه ويلي عليه ولاية كاملة مطلقة. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٩/٢] وأولاده الصغار: قيد بالإضافة، ولم يقل: والصغار لإخراج الصغير الأجنبي إذا مانه؛ فإن صدقة الفطر لا تجب، وأطلق "أولاده" فشمل الذكر والأنثى للعلة المذكورة، وهو وجوب نفقته عليه وثبوت الولاية الكاملة عليه، فاستفيد منه أن البنت الصغيرة إذا زوجت وسلّمت إلى الزوج، ثم جاء يوم الفطر لا يجب على الأب صدقة فطرها؛ لعدم المؤنة عليه، وشمل الولد بين الأبوين، فإن على كل واحد منهما صدقة تامة، وقيد بالفقر؛ لأن الولد الغني بملك نصاب تجب صدقة فطره في ماله [البحر الرائق بتصرف وتغير: ٣٩٩/٢] على الجد قال في "البحر": وخرج ولد الولد فإن صدقة فطره لا تجب على جده عند عدم أبيه أو فقره على ظاهر الرواية؛ لعدم الولاية المطلقة، فإن ولايته ناقصة لانتقالها إليه من الأب، فصارت كولاية الوصي، وتعقبه في "فتح القدير" بالفرق بين الجد والوصي لوجوب النفقة على الجد دون الوصي، فلم يبق إلا مجرد انتقال الولاية، ولا أثر له بالفرق بين الجد والوصي كمشتري العبد، ولا مخلص إلا بترجيح رواية الحسن أن على الجد صدقة فطرهم. وهذه مسائل يخالف فيها الجد الأب في ظاهر الرواية، ولا يخالف في رواية الحسن هذه. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٩/١]
Chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr. It is obligatory upon a free Muslim who owns a nisab or its value, even if a year has not passed, at the time of dawn of the day of Fitr, and it was not for trade, free from debt and his basic needs and the needs of his family. What is considered? Even if it is not the description of nisab, it is sufficiency, not estimation. They are his dwelling, furnishings, clothes, horse, weapon, and slaves for service; it is household goods. He pays it for himself and his minor poor children. If they are wealthy, he pays from their wealth. It is not obligatory upon the grandfather in the apparent narration, but it is chosen that the grandfather is like the father when the father is absent or poor. Conditions: free, Muslim, ownership of nisab: as explained. Know that the nisabs are three: a nisab in which growth is required and zakat and other rulings related to growing wealth apply; a nisab by which four rulings become obligatory: prohibition of charity, obligation of sacrifice, sadaqat al-fitr, and maintenance of relatives, and growth by trade nor the passing of a year is required; and a nisab by which the prohibition of asking is established: it is when one has his day's sustenance according to some, and some said: it is to own fifty dirhams. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi with addition: 723] At the time of dawn etc.: explanation of the time of obligation for payment. It is accusative as an adverb for 'obligatory' at the beginning of the chapter. Whoever dies before dawn or is born or accepts Islam after it, it is not obligatory upon him. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 2/141] For himself: beginning of explanation of the cause: it is himself and those in his meaning whom he supports and over whom he has complete and absolute guardianship. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/399] And his minor children: restriction by addition; he did not say 'and the minor' to exclude a foreign minor if he supports him, for sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory. He said 'his children' generally, including male and female for the mentioned reason: the obligation of their maintenance upon him and the establishment of complete guardianship. From this it is understood that if a minor daughter is married and handed over to her husband, then the day of Fitr comes, it is not obligatory upon the father to pay her sadaqat al-fitr because he does not have maintenance of her. It includes the child between two parents, for each of them is required to pay a complete sadaqah. It is restricted by poverty because if the child is wealthy owning a nisab, his sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory from his own wealth. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification and change 2/399] Regarding the grandfather, it is said in al-Bahr: the child's child is excluded, for his sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory upon his grandfather when his father is absent or poor according to the apparent narration because there is no absolute guardianship; his guardianship is incomplete because it transfers to him from the father, so it becomes like the guardianship of a executor. It was objected in Fath al-Qadir by distinguishing between the grandfather and the executor due to the obligation of maintenance upon the grandfather but not the executor. Nothing remains except mere transfer of guardianship, and it has no effect by the distinction between the grandfather and the executor like the purchaser of a slave. There is no escape except by preferring the narration of Hasan that the grandfather is obligated for their sadaqat al-fitr. These are issues in which the grandfather differs from the father in the apparent narration, and he does not differ in this narration of Hasan. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/399]
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr. It states that it is obligatory (tajibu) upon every free Muslim who owns a nisab (minimum wealth threshold) or its value, even if a full year has not passed, at the time of dawn of the day of Eid al-Fitr. The wealth must not be for trade. The person must be free from debt and basic needs (such as dwelling, furnishings, clothes, horse, weapon, slaves for service—these are considered household goods and not part of the nisab). The person pays for himself and his minor poor children. If the children are wealthy, he pays from their wealth. It is not obligatory upon the grandfather according to the apparent narration, but a chosen view equates the grandfather to the father when the father is absent or poor. The commentary explains the conditions: freedom ensures valid transfer of ownership; Islam ensures worship; ownership of nisab is necessary because the obligation aims to enrich the poor, which can only be done by the wealthy. There are three types of nisab: (1) nisab that requires growth (like zakat on trade goods), (2) nisab that triggers four rulings: prohibition of receiving charity, obligation of sacrifice, sadaqat al-fitr, and maintenance of relatives—this does not require growth or a full year, and (3) nisab for the prohibition of asking (some say a day's food, others say fifty dirhams). The time of obligation is dawn of Eid; if a person dies before dawn or a child is born after dawn or a person accepts Islam after dawn, sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory for them. For oneself: the cause includes oneself and anyone for whom one has full maintenance and absolute guardianship. For minor children: the word 'his children' includes male and female, and the condition of poverty applies because if they are wealthy, they pay from their own wealth. If a minor daughter is married and handed over to her husband, her father is not obligated because he no longer maintains her. For a child with two parents, each parent must pay a full sadaqah. The passage then discusses the grandfather: according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), he is not obligated for his grandchildren's sadaqat al-fitr because his guardianship is incomplete (transferred from the father, like that of an executor). However, Fath al-Qadir distinguishes between a grandfather and an executor because the grandfather is obligated for maintenance, while the executor is not. Nevertheless, the only escape is to prefer the narration of Hasan (ibn Ziyad) that the grandfather is obligated. This highlights an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf where the grandfather differs from the father in the apparent narration but agrees in Hasan's narration.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): Minimum wealth threshold; for Zakat al-Fitr, sufficiency beyond basic needs, no growth required.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The established transmission from Abu Hanifa and his students in the six books of Imam Muhammad.
صغار (sighar): Minors, children who have not reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Being free
B) Being Muslim
C) Owning nisab that has been in possession for a full lunar year
D) Being alive at dawn of Eid

Key Takeaway
Zakat al-Fitr is wajib upon every free Muslim with nisab at dawn of Eid, covering himself and his minor poor children.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate

The Grandfather’s Obligation and the Three Nisabs
Dispute on grandfather and classification of nisabs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on grandfather's obligation for grandchildren's Zakat al-Fitr and distinguish the three types of nisab.

Core Idea
Grandfather is not obligated in zahir al-riwayah, but the chosen view equates him to the father when the father is absent or poor.

This lesson delves into a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the grandfather's liability for Zakat al-Fitr of his grandchildren. The passage presents both the position of the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) and the chosen view (ikhtiyar) supported by a narration from Hasan al-Shaybani. Additionally, the commentary provides a valuable classification of the three types of nisab, each with different legal implications. Understanding these nuances is crucial for accurate fatwa and practice.

Guided Path
1
State the position of Zahir al-Riwayah
Identify that according to the established transmission (Abu Hanifa and his two students), the grandfather is not obligated to pay Zakat al-Fitr for his grandchildren, even if the father is absent or poor. The reason given is the lack of absolute guardianship.
The text: 'ولا تجب على الجد في ظاهر الرواية'.
2
Identify the opposing narration and its basis
Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated that the grandfather is obligated like the father. The objection in Fath al-Qadir is that the grandfather differs from a guardian (wasii) because he is obligated for maintenance, suggesting his guardianship is stronger.
The chosen view (ikhtiyar) accepts this narration.
3
Understand the tarjih mechanism
The text says 'there is no escape except by preferring the narration of Hasan'. The tarjih authority (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Fath al-Qadir) weighs the maintenance obligation against the transfer of guardianship.
This is an intra-Hanafi tarjih where a weaker narration is preferred due to practical reasoning.
4
Distinguish the three nisabs
Identify: (1) Nisab requiring growth – for zakat and trade. (2) Nisab for four rulings – including Zakat al-Fitr – no growth or year needed. (3) Nisab for prohibition of asking – a day's food or fifty dirhams.
Zakat al-Fitr belongs to the second type, which makes it easier to trigger than zakat.
5
Apply the ikhtilaf to a scenario
Consider a grandfather whose son is deceased, with a minor grandchild. According to the chosen view, the grandfather must pay. According to zahir al-riwayah, he would not. Currently, the mufta bihi is the chosen view.
Know the practical consequence (thamarah) of the dispute.

Source Text

باب صدقة الفطر
tجب على حر مسلم مالك لنصاب أو قيمته وإن لم يحل عليه الحول، عند طلوع فجر يوم الفطر، ولم يكن للتجارة، فارغ عن الدين وحاجته الأصلية وحوائج عياله، والمعتبر أي وإن لم يكن نعت نصاب في فيها الكفاية لا التقدير، وهي مسكنه وأثاثه وثيابه وفرسه وسلاحه وعبيده للخدمة، هو متاع البيت فيخرجها عن نفسه وأولاده الصغار الفقراء، وإن كانوا أغنياء يخرجها من مالهم، ولا تجب على الجد في ظاهر الرواية، واختير أن الجد كالأب عند فقده أو فقره الأب
على حر مسلم إلخ شرط الحرية، ليتحقق التمليك، فلا تجب على العبد، والإسلام؛ لتقع قربة، فلا تجب على الكافر، وملك النصاب؛ لأنها وجبت لإغناء الفقير، والإغناء من غير الغني لا يكون. اعلم أن النصب ثلاثة: نصاب يشترط فيه النماء، وتتعلق به الزكاة وسائر الأحكام المتعلقة بالمال النامي. ونصاب تجب به أحكام أربعة حرمة الصدقة، ووجوب الأضحية، وصدقة الفطر، ونفقة الأقارب، ولا يشترط فيه النمو بالتجارة ولا حولان الحول ونصاب تثبت به حرمة السؤال، وهو ما إذا كان عنده قوت يومه عند بعض، وقال بعضهم : هو أن يملك خمسين درهما. [حاشية الطحطاوي بزيادة: ٧٢٣] عند طلوع إلخ بيان لوقت وجوب أدائها، وهو منصوب وعلى أنه ظرف؛ لـ "تجب" أول الباب، فمن مات قبل طلوع الفجر أو ولد أو أسلم بعده لا تجب عليه [تبيين الحقائق: ١٤١/٢] عن نفسه: شروع في بيان السبب، وهو رأسه، وما كان في معناه ممن يمونه ويلي عليه ولاية كاملة مطلقة. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٩/٢] وأولاده الصغار: قيد بالإضافة، ولم يقل: والصغار لإخراج الصغير الأجنبي إذا مانه؛ فإن صدقة الفطر لا تجب، وأطلق "أولاده" فشمل الذكر والأنثى للعلة المذكورة، وهو وجوب نفقته عليه وثبوت الولاية الكاملة عليه، فاستفيد منه أن البنت الصغيرة إذا زوجت وسلّمت إلى الزوج، ثم جاء يوم الفطر لا يجب على الأب صدقة فطرها؛ لعدم المؤنة عليه، وشمل الولد بين الأبوين، فإن على كل واحد منهما صدقة تامة، وقيد بالفقر؛ لأن الولد الغني بملك نصاب تجب صدقة فطره في ماله [البحر الرائق بتصرف وتغير: ٣٩٩/٢] على الجد قال في "البحر": وخرج ولد الولد فإن صدقة فطره لا تجب على جده عند عدم أبيه أو فقره على ظاهر الرواية؛ لعدم الولاية المطلقة، فإن ولايته ناقصة لانتقالها إليه من الأب، فصارت كولاية الوصي، وتعقبه في "فتح القدير" بالفرق بين الجد والوصي لوجوب النفقة على الجد دون الوصي، فلم يبق إلا مجرد انتقال الولاية، ولا أثر له بالفرق بين الجد والوصي كمشتري العبد، ولا مخلص إلا بترجيح رواية الحسن أن على الجد صدقة فطرهم. وهذه مسائل يخالف فيها الجد الأب في ظاهر الرواية، ولا يخالف في رواية الحسن هذه. [البحر الرائق: ٣٩٩/١]
Chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr. It is obligatory upon a free Muslim who owns a nisab or its value, even if a year has not passed, at the time of dawn of the day of Fitr, and it was not for trade, free from debt and his basic needs and the needs of his family. What is considered? Even if it is not the description of nisab, it is sufficiency, not estimation. They are his dwelling, furnishings, clothes, horse, weapon, and slaves for service; it is household goods. He pays it for himself and his minor poor children. If they are wealthy, he pays from their wealth. It is not obligatory upon the grandfather in the apparent narration, but it is chosen that the grandfather is like the father when the father is absent or poor. Conditions: free, Muslim, ownership of nisab: as explained. Know that the nisabs are three: a nisab in which growth is required and zakat and other rulings related to growing wealth apply; a nisab by which four rulings become obligatory: prohibition of charity, obligation of sacrifice, sadaqat al-fitr, and maintenance of relatives, and growth by trade nor the passing of a year is required; and a nisab by which the prohibition of asking is established: it is when one has his day's sustenance according to some, and some said: it is to own fifty dirhams. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi with addition: 723] At the time of dawn etc.: explanation of the time of obligation for payment. It is accusative as an adverb for 'obligatory' at the beginning of the chapter. Whoever dies before dawn or is born or accepts Islam after it, it is not obligatory upon him. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 2/141] For himself: beginning of explanation of the cause: it is himself and those in his meaning whom he supports and over whom he has complete and absolute guardianship. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/399] And his minor children: restriction by addition; he did not say 'and the minor' to exclude a foreign minor if he supports him, for sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory. He said 'his children' generally, including male and female for the mentioned reason: the obligation of their maintenance upon him and the establishment of complete guardianship. From this it is understood that if a minor daughter is married and handed over to her husband, then the day of Fitr comes, it is not obligatory upon the father to pay her sadaqat al-fitr because he does not have maintenance of her. It includes the child between two parents, for each of them is required to pay a complete sadaqah. It is restricted by poverty because if the child is wealthy owning a nisab, his sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory from his own wealth. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification and change 2/399] Regarding the grandfather, it is said in al-Bahr: the child's child is excluded, for his sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory upon his grandfather when his father is absent or poor according to the apparent narration because there is no absolute guardianship; his guardianship is incomplete because it transfers to him from the father, so it becomes like the guardianship of a executor. It was objected in Fath al-Qadir by distinguishing between the grandfather and the executor due to the obligation of maintenance upon the grandfather but not the executor. Nothing remains except mere transfer of guardianship, and it has no effect by the distinction between the grandfather and the executor like the purchaser of a slave. There is no escape except by preferring the narration of Hasan that the grandfather is obligated for their sadaqat al-fitr. These are issues in which the grandfather differs from the father in the apparent narration, and he does not differ in this narration of Hasan. [al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2/399]
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr. It states that it is obligatory (tajibu) upon every free Muslim who owns a nisab (minimum wealth threshold) or its value, even if a full year has not passed, at the time of dawn of the day of Eid al-Fitr. The wealth must not be for trade. The person must be free from debt and basic needs (such as dwelling, furnishings, clothes, horse, weapon, slaves for service—these are considered household goods and not part of the nisab). The person pays for himself and his minor poor children. If the children are wealthy, he pays from their wealth. It is not obligatory upon the grandfather according to the apparent narration, but a chosen view equates the grandfather to the father when the father is absent or poor. The commentary explains the conditions: freedom ensures valid transfer of ownership; Islam ensures worship; ownership of nisab is necessary because the obligation aims to enrich the poor, which can only be done by the wealthy. There are three types of nisab: (1) nisab that requires growth (like zakat on trade goods), (2) nisab that triggers four rulings: prohibition of receiving charity, obligation of sacrifice, sadaqat al-fitr, and maintenance of relatives—this does not require growth or a full year, and (3) nisab for the prohibition of asking (some say a day's food, others say fifty dirhams). The time of obligation is dawn of Eid; if a person dies before dawn or a child is born after dawn or a person accepts Islam after dawn, sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory for them. For oneself: the cause includes oneself and anyone for whom one has full maintenance and absolute guardianship. For minor children: the word 'his children' includes male and female, and the condition of poverty applies because if they are wealthy, they pay from their own wealth. If a minor daughter is married and handed over to her husband, her father is not obligated because he no longer maintains her. For a child with two parents, each parent must pay a full sadaqah. The passage then discusses the grandfather: according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), he is not obligated for his grandchildren's sadaqat al-fitr because his guardianship is incomplete (transferred from the father, like that of an executor). However, Fath al-Qadir distinguishes between a grandfather and an executor because the grandfather is obligated for maintenance, while the executor is not. Nevertheless, the only escape is to prefer the narration of Hasan (ibn Ziyad) that the grandfather is obligated. This highlights an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf where the grandfather differs from the father in the apparent narration but agrees in Hasan's narration.

Key Terms
الجد (al-jadd): Grandfather; in this context, paternal grandfather (or maternal if no paternal).
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): Established transmission from the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
النصب ثلاثة (al-nusub thalatha): The nisabs are three types, each with different legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (mufta bihi), when is the grandfather obligated to pay Zakat al-Fitr for his grandchildren?
A) Always, regardless of father's status.
B) Only when the father is deceased.
C) When the father is absent or poor.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that the grandfather must pay Zakat al-Fitr for his grandchildren when the father is absent or poor, based on the narration of Hasan.

Zakat — Almsgiving
beginner
8 min
Conditions of Obligation for Sadaqat al-Fitr
Ruling statement on conditions of obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions of obligation for sadaqat al-fitr and explain why nisab is defined as kifayah (sufficiency) rather than a fixed amount.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-fitr is wajib on every free Muslim who possesses basic sufficiency beyond needs at dawn of Eid.

This lesson unpacks the conditions that trigger the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr. It is crucial to understand who must pay and why before learning the amounts and distribution. The Hanafi school defines nisab here as kifayah, meaning one has enough to cover basic needs and still has surplus on the day of Eid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory (tajibu) on a free Muslim who owns nisab. Note the Hanafi position: it is wajib, not fard, due to the nature of its evidence.
Wajib vs fard: both are binding, but wajib is proven by zanni evidence, fard by qati evidence.
2
List the three personal conditions
The three conditions are: (1) freedom (hurr), (2) Islam (muslim), (3) possession of nisab (malik li-nisab). The passage explicitly negates obligation on a slave and a disbeliever.
Freedom is required to ensure ownership; Islam for the act to be an act of worship (qurbah).
3
Define nisab for sadaqat al-fitr
The passage states 'al-mu'tabar fi al-nisab al-kifayah la al-taqdir' – what is considered is sufficiency, not a fixed estimation. It then lists basic needs: dwelling, furniture, clothes, horse, weapon, slaves for service. One must have these plus surplus for fitr.
This differs from zakat nisab which is a fixed amount (200 dirhams) and requires growth.
4
Identify the time condition
Obligation is tied to the dawn (tulu' fajr) of the day of Fitr (Eid al-Fitr). If someone dies, becomes Muslim, or is born after that time, the obligation is not incurred.
The passage later discusses early payment but the obligation attaches at dawn.
5
Distinguish from other nisabs
The passage mentions three types of nisab. One for zakat (requires growth and year), one for fitr/udhiyah/maintenance (no growth or year), and one for prohibition of asking (food for a day or 50 dirhams).
This classification helps understand the different thresholds in fiqh.

The Book of Zakat. Chapter of Sadaqat al-Fitr. It is obligatory upon a free Muslim who owns nisab or its value, even if the year has not passed over it, at the dawn of the day of Fitr, and it is not for trade, free from debt and his basic needs and needs of his dependents. What is considered in nisab is sufficiency, not estimation. They are: his dwelling, furniture, clothes, horse, weapon, and slaves for service. He pays it for himself and his young poor children. If they are rich, he pays it from their wealth. It is not obligatory upon the grandfather in the apparent narration, and it is chosen that the grandfather is like the father when the father is absent or poor. The father: 'upon a free Muslim etc.' condition of freedom to realize ownership, so it is not obligatory upon a slave; and Islam to be an act of worship, so it is not obligatory upon a disbeliever; and possession of nisab because it was obligated to enrich the poor, and enrichment cannot be from other than the rich. Know that the nisabs are three: a nisab in which growth is conditioned, and zakat and other rulings related to growing wealth are attached to it. And a nisab by which four rulings are obligated: prohibition of charity, obligation of udhiyah, sadaqat al-fitr, and maintenance of relatives, and growth by trade is not conditioned in it nor the passing of the year. And a nisab by which the prohibition of asking is established: it is when one has food for his day according to some, and some said: it is that he possesses fifty dirhams. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi with addition: 723] 'At the dawn etc.' is an explanation of the time of its obligation, and it is in the accusative as an adverb for 'it is obligatory' at the beginning of the chapter. So whoever dies before dawn or is born or becomes Muslim after it, it is not obligatory upon him. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 2/141] 'For himself': beginning of explanation of the reason, which is himself and what is in his meaning of those whom he supports and over whom he has complete absolute guardianship. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 2/399] 'And his young children': restricted by the addition, and he did not say 'and the young' to exclude the young unrelated person if he supports him; then sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory. And he generalizes 'his children' so it includes male and female for the mentioned reason, which is the obligation of maintenance upon him and the establishment of complete guardianship over them. So it is understood from this that if the young daughter is married and handed over to the husband, then the day of Fitr comes, it is not obligatory upon the father to give her sadaqat al-fitr due to lack of maintenance. And it includes the child between two parents, then upon each of them is a complete sadaqah. And he restricted it to poverty because the rich child who possesses nisab has his sadaqat al-fitr obligatory in his wealth. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification and change: 2/399] 'Upon the grandfather': He said in al-Bahr: And he excluded the child's child, for his sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory upon his grandfather when his father is absent or poor according to the apparent narration, due to lack of absolute guardianship, for his guardianship is incomplete because it transfers to him from the father, so it becomes like the guardianship of a guardian. And he criticized it in Fath al-Qadir by differentiating between the grandfather and the guardian due to the obligation of maintenance on the grandfather without the guardian, so nothing remains except mere transfer of guardianship, and it has no effect. The distinction between the grandfather and the guardian is like the buyer of a slave. There is no escape except by preferring the narration of al-Hasan that upon the grandfather is their sadaqat al-fitr. These are issues in which the grandfather differs from the father in the apparent narration, and he does not differ in this narration of al-Hasan. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 1/399] 240 Sadaqat al-Fitr The Book of Zakat And for his slaves for service, his mudabbar, and his umm walad, even if they are disbelievers; not for his mukatib, nor for his adult child, nor his wife, nor a jointly owned slave, nor a runaway slave except after his return; likewise the usurped and the captive. And it is half a sa' of wheat, or its flour, or its groats; or a sa' of dates, raisins, or barley; which is eight ratls by Iraqi measure. And it is permissible to give value, and it is better when one finds what he needs because it is quicker to fulfill the poor person's need. If it is a time of hardship, then wheat, barley, and what is eaten are better than dirhams. The time of obligation is at the dawn of the day of Fitr. So whoever dies or becomes poor before it, or becomes Muslim, or becomes rich, or is born after it, it is not obligatory upon him. It is recommended to give it before going out to the prayer ground. It is valid if one gives it early or delayed, but delaying is disliked. Each person gives his fitr to one poor person. There is disagreement on the permissibility of dividing one fitr among more than one poor person. It is permissible to give what is upon a group to one person according to the correct view. And Allah is the granter of success. And for his slaves for service: He generalizes it so it includes the indebted, hired, and mortgaged slave if he has the means to pay the debt; the slave who commits an offense intentionally or accidentally; the slave vowed to be given in charity; the slave whose emancipation is suspended upon the arrival of the day of Fitr; and the slave whose corpus is bequeathed to one person and his service to another; then it (the fitr) is upon the one who is bequeathed the corpus, unlike maintenance which is upon the one who is bequeathed the service. And he indicated by his saying 'for service' that he does not give fitr for his runaway slave, nor for the usurped slave that is denied, except after his return, and then it is incumbent for the past; nor for his captive slave because it is out of his hand and control, so he resembles the mukatib; nor for his servant by hire or loan. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification: 1/400] And his mudabbar: mudabbar is a slave to whom his master said: 'You are free after my death,' for example. And his umm walad: umm walad is a slave woman who bears a child from her master, and the master claims the child's lineage. Even if they are disbelievers: meaning even if these are disbelievers. And a jointly owned slave: meaning he does not give fitr for a slave between two or more partners. 'What he needs': meaning from these types from which fitr is given, if the time is a time of hardship. 'And it is valid if given early': He indicated by its generality that there is no distinction between one period and another as in al-Hidayah, and this is correct. According to Khalaf ibn Ayyub, it is permissible to give it early after the beginning of Ramadan, not before. It is said it is permissible in the last half of Ramadan. It is said in the last ten days. According to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, it is not permissible to give it early at all, like udhiyah, and it is invalidated by the passing of the day of Fitr because it is an act of worship specific to the day of Eid, so it is invalidated by its passing, like udhiyah is invalidated by the passing of the days of Nahr. We say: It is a financial act of worship that is not invalidated after obligation except by payment, like zakat; and udhiyah is not invalidated but the obligation transfers to giving in charity with its value; this is because the act of worship in shedding blood is not rationally understood, but is known by law in specific days, while the aspect of worship in giving charity is rational, supplying the need of the needy, so the time of performance is not restricted to a specific time. [Al-Kifayah with addition: 2/232] And it is
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr from Nur al-Idah, a matn in Hanafi fiqh. It presents the rulings of sadaqat al-fitr in a concise manner suitable for memorization. The rulings are stated without proofs, though the accompanying commentary provides some rational justifications. The passage covers the conditions of obligation: free Muslim, owner of nisab (beyond basic needs), time at dawn of Eid al-Fitr. It defines nisab as sufficiency, listing basic needs. It then discusses on whose behalf one pays: self, young poor children, and the dispute regarding grandchildren. The grandfather issue is detailed: in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), the grandfather does not pay for grandchildren, but there is a preferred narration from Imam Muhammad that he does. Next, the passage lists for whom one pays from his slaves: slaves for service, mudabbar, umm walad (even if non-Muslim), but not for mukatib, adult child, wife, jointly owned slave, runaway, usurped, or captive. The amount is half a sa' of wheat or its products, or one sa' of dates, raisins, or barley (8 Iraqi ratls). Paying the value is permissible and preferred except in times of hardship. The time of obligation is dawn of Eid; those who die, become poor, embrace Islam, become rich, or are born after that are not obligated. It is recommended to pay before going to the prayer ground; early payment is valid, delay is disliked. Distribution: one person's fitr to one poor person is the norm; dividing one fitr among multiple poor is disputed; paying multiple fitrs to one person is permissible. The commentary elaborates on each point, including definitions and exceptions.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that makes one liable for sadaqat al-fitr. Here, it means sufficiency beyond basic needs, not a fixed monetary threshold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the nisab for sadaqat al-fitr according to the Hanafi school?
A) 200 dirhams of silver or 20 mithqal of gold
B) Ownership of a house and furniture
C) Sufficiency (kifayah) beyond basic needs and debts
D) Having food for one year

Key Takeaway
Sadaqat al-fitr is wajib on every free Muslim who, after covering basic needs and debts, has sufficient surplus at dawn of Eid.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
10 min
On Whose Behalf? Self, Children, Dependents, and the Grandfather Dispute
Ruling statement on dependents and grandfather dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of people for whom one must pay sadaqat al-fitr and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the grandfather's obligation for grandchildren.

Core Idea
One pays for himself, his young poor children, and his slaves; the grandfather's obligation is disputed.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage: determining on whose behalf the head of the household must pay sadaqat al-fitr. The rulings include oneself, young poor children, and various types of slaves. A significant intra-madhhab dispute arises regarding the grandfather: does he pay for grandchildren when the father is absent or poor? We will trace the reasoning behind the two positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling: for oneself
The passage explicitly states 'yukhrijuhu `an nafsih' – he pays for himself. This is the primary obligation before considering others.
2
Understand the ruling for young poor children
He pays for his young (minor) children who are poor. If they are rich, he pays from their own wealth. The condition of poverty is tied to the child being poor; if the child has wealth, the fitr is taken from that wealth.
The phrase 'wa awladihi al-sighar al-fuqara' limits to poor minors.
3
Examine the exception for married daughters
The commentary explains that if a young daughter is married and handed over to the husband, the father does not pay her fitr because the maintenance responsibility has transferred to the husband.
This shows the link between maintenance (nafaqah) and fitr obligation.
4
Analyze the grandfather dispute
The text first states 'wa la tajibu `ala al-jadd fi zahir al-riwayah' – not obligatory on grandfather in the apparent narration. Then it says 'wa ukhtira anna al-jadd ka al-ab `inda faqdihi aw faqr al-ab' – but it is chosen that the grandfather is like the father when the father is absent or poor. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad supports the grandfather's obligation, while the zahir al-riwayah (from Imam Muhammad's six books) denies it. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows the narration of al-Hasan.
The commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the reasoning based on guardianship and maintenance.
5
List the slaves for whom one pays
The passage lists: slaves for service, mudabbar, umm walad (even if disbelievers). It excludes: mukatib, adult child, wife, jointly owned slave, runaway slave, usurped, captive. Also excludes hired or borrowed servants.
The distinction is based on ownership and control.

The Book of Zakat. Chapter of Sadaqat al-Fitr. It is obligatory upon a free Muslim who owns nisab or its value, even if the year has not passed over it, at the dawn of the day of Fitr, and it is not for trade, free from debt and his basic needs and needs of his dependents. What is considered in nisab is sufficiency, not estimation. They are: his dwelling, furniture, clothes, horse, weapon, and slaves for service. He pays it for himself and his young poor children. If they are rich, he pays it from their wealth. It is not obligatory upon the grandfather in the apparent narration, and it is chosen that the grandfather is like the father when the father is absent or poor. The father: 'upon a free Muslim etc.' condition of freedom to realize ownership, so it is not obligatory upon a slave; and Islam to be an act of worship, so it is not obligatory upon a disbeliever; and possession of nisab because it was obligated to enrich the poor, and enrichment cannot be from other than the rich. Know that the nisabs are three: a nisab in which growth is conditioned, and zakat and other rulings related to growing wealth are attached to it. And a nisab by which four rulings are obligated: prohibition of charity, obligation of udhiyah, sadaqat al-fitr, and maintenance of relatives, and growth by trade is not conditioned in it nor the passing of the year. And a nisab by which the prohibition of asking is established: it is when one has food for his day according to some, and some said: it is that he possesses fifty dirhams. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi with addition: 723] 'At the dawn etc.' is an explanation of the time of its obligation, and it is in the accusative as an adverb for 'it is obligatory' at the beginning of the chapter. So whoever dies before dawn or is born or becomes Muslim after it, it is not obligatory upon him. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 2/141] 'For himself': beginning of explanation of the reason, which is himself and what is in his meaning of those whom he supports and over whom he has complete absolute guardianship. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 2/399] 'And his young children': restricted by the addition, and he did not say 'and the young' to exclude the young unrelated person if he supports him; then sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory. And he generalizes 'his children' so it includes male and female for the mentioned reason, which is the obligation of maintenance upon him and the establishment of complete guardianship over them. So it is understood from this that if the young daughter is married and handed over to the husband, then the day of Fitr comes, it is not obligatory upon the father to give her sadaqat al-fitr due to lack of maintenance. And it includes the child between two parents, then upon each of them is a complete sadaqah. And he restricted it to poverty because the rich child who possesses nisab has his sadaqat al-fitr obligatory in his wealth. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification and change: 2/399] 'Upon the grandfather': He said in al-Bahr: And he excluded the child's child, for his sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory upon his grandfather when his father is absent or poor according to the apparent narration, due to lack of absolute guardianship, for his guardianship is incomplete because it transfers to him from the father, so it becomes like the guardianship of a guardian. And he criticized it in Fath al-Qadir by differentiating between the grandfather and the guardian due to the obligation of maintenance on the grandfather without the guardian, so nothing remains except mere transfer of guardianship, and it has no effect. The distinction between the grandfather and the guardian is like the buyer of a slave. There is no escape except by preferring the narration of al-Hasan that upon the grandfather is their sadaqat al-fitr. These are issues in which the grandfather differs from the father in the apparent narration, and he does not differ in this narration of al-Hasan. Commentary
This passage is the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr from Nur al-Idah, a matn in Hanafi fiqh. It presents the rulings of sadaqat al-fitr in a concise manner suitable for memorization. The rulings are stated without proofs, though the accompanying commentary provides some rational justifications. The passage covers the conditions of obligation: free Muslim, owner of nisab (beyond basic needs), time at dawn of Eid al-Fitr. It defines nisab as sufficiency, listing basic needs. It then discusses on whose behalf one pays: self, young poor children, and the dispute regarding grandchildren. The grandfather issue is detailed: in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), the grandfather does not pay for grandchildren, but there is a preferred narration from Imam Muhammad that he does. Next, the passage lists for whom one pays from his slaves: slaves for service, mudabbar, umm walad (even if non-Muslim), but not for mukatib, adult child, wife, jointly owned slave, runaway, usurped, or captive. The amount is half a sa' of wheat or its products, or one sa' of dates, raisins, or barley (8 Iraqi ratls). Paying the value is permissible and preferred except in times of hardship. The time of obligation is dawn of Eid; those who die, become poor, embrace Islam, become rich, or are born after that are not obligated. It is recommended to pay before going to the prayer ground; early payment is valid, delay is disliked. Distribution: one person's fitr to one poor person is the norm; dividing one fitr among multiple poor is disputed; paying multiple fitrs to one person is permissible. The commentary elaborates on each point, including definitions and exceptions.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent narration from Imam Muhammad's six books, representing the standard Hanafi position.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been told by his master 'you are free after my death'.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child from her master and the master acknowledges the child's lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, when must the grandfather pay sadaqat al-fitr for his grandchildren?
A) Always, regardless of the father's situation
B) Only when the father is absent or poor
C) Never, because his guardianship is incomplete
D) Only if the grandchildren are very young

Key Takeaway
One pays sadaqat al-fitr for oneself, minor poor children, and owned slaves; the grandfather's obligation for grandchildren is the chosen view when the father is unable.

Zakat — Almsgiving
beginner
8 min
Amount, Timing, and Distribution of Sadaqat al-Fitr
Ruling statement on amount, timing, and distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the amount of sadaqat al-fitr in different types of food, explain the ruling on paying value, describe the time of obligation and recommended time for distribution, and summarize the rules for distributing fitr to multiple recipients.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-fitr is half a sa' of wheat or one sa' of other grains; cash value is preferred except in hardship.

This final lesson from the passage covers the practical specifics: what quantity to give, whether to give food or cash, when the obligation arises and when it is best to distribute, and how many poor recipients each fitr should go to. These details are essential for proper observance of this wajib act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic amount
The passage states: half a sa' of wheat (burr) or its flour or groats (sawiq), or one sa' of dates (tamr), raisins (zabib), or barley (sha'ir). One sa' is eight ratls by Iraqi measure.
These are the standard types of staple food in the classical context.
2
Understand the ruling on paying value (qimah)
It is permissible to pay the cash value (yuqim) instead of the food items. In normal times, paying value is better (afdal) because it more quickly satisfies the poor's needs. However, in times of hardship (zaman shiddah), it is better to give the actual grains (wheat, barley) because they are more beneficial.
This flexibility is a Hanafi feature; some schools require the specific food items.
3
Know the time of obligation and recommended time
The obligation attaches at dawn on the day of Eid (Fitr). Those who die, become poor, embrace Islam, become rich, or are born after that are not obligated. It is recommended (mustahabb) to pay before going to the Eid prayer ground. Early payment before Eid is valid (sahih), and delaying after Eid is valid but disliked (makruh). The obligation does not drop by passing the day; it remains as a debt.
The passage and commentary stress that early payment is allowed without restriction, contrary to some other views.
4
Learn the distribution rules
Each person's fitr should be given to a single poor person (yadfa`u kull shakhs fitratahu li-faqir wahid). There is disagreement on dividing one fitr among multiple poor people, but it is permissible to give the fitr of multiple people to a single poor person according to the correct view (sahih).
This ensures the poor receive enough to be enriched.
5
Review the exceptions for slaves and captives
The passage reiterates that one does not pay for runaway, usurped, or captive slaves until they return. It also excludes hired or borrowed servants.
This links back to the previous lesson's categories.

The Book of Zakat. Chapter of Sadaqat al-Fitr. It is obligatory upon a free Muslim who owns nisab or its value, even if the year has not passed over it, at the dawn of the day of Fitr, and it is not for trade, free from debt and his basic needs and needs of his dependents. What is considered in nisab is sufficiency, not estimation. They are: his dwelling, furniture, clothes, horse, weapon, and slaves for service. He pays it for himself and his young poor children. If they are rich, he pays it from their wealth. It is not obligatory upon the grandfather in the apparent narration, and it is chosen that the grandfather is like the father when the father is absent or poor. The father: 'upon a free Muslim etc.' condition of freedom to realize ownership, so it is not obligatory upon a slave; and Islam to be an act of worship, so it is not obligatory upon a disbeliever; and possession of nisab because it was obligated to enrich the poor, and enrichment cannot be from other than the rich. Know that the nisabs are three: a nisab in which growth is conditioned, and zakat and other rulings related to growing wealth are attached to it. And a nisab by which four rulings are obligated: prohibition of charity, obligation of udhiyah, sadaqat al-fitr, and maintenance of relatives, and growth by trade is not conditioned in it nor the passing of the year. And a nisab by which the prohibition of asking is established: it is when one has food for his day according to some, and some said: it is that he possesses fifty dirhams. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi with addition: 723] 'At the dawn etc.' is an explanation of the time of its obligation, and it is in the accusative as an adverb for 'it is obligatory' at the beginning of the chapter. So whoever dies before dawn or is born or becomes Muslim after it, it is not obligatory upon him. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 2/141] 'For himself': beginning of explanation of the reason, which is himself and what is in his meaning of those whom he supports and over whom he has complete absolute guardianship. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 2/399] 'And his young children': restricted by the addition, and he did not say 'and the young' to exclude the young unrelated person if he supports him; then sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory. And he generalizes 'his children' so it includes male and female for the mentioned reason, which is the obligation of maintenance upon him and the establishment of complete guardianship over them. So it is understood from this that if the young daughter is married and handed over to the husband, then the day of Fitr comes, it is not obligatory upon the father to give her sadaqat al-fitr due to lack of maintenance. And it includes the child between two parents, then upon each of them is a complete sadaqah. And he restricted it to poverty because the rich child who possesses nisab has his sadaqat al-fitr obligatory in his wealth. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification and change: 2/399] 'Upon the grandfather': He said in al-Bahr: And he excluded the child's child, for his sadaqat al-fitr is not obligatory upon his grandfather when his father is absent or poor according to the apparent narration, due to lack of absolute guardianship, for his guardianship is incomplete because it transfers to him from the father, so it becomes like the guardianship of a guardian. And he criticized it in Fath al-Qadir by differentiating between the grandfather and the guardian due to the obligation of maintenance on the grandfather without the guardian, so nothing remains except mere transfer of guardianship, and it has no effect. The distinction between the grandfather and the guardian is like the buyer of a slave. There is no escape except by preferring the narration of al-Hasan that upon the grandfather is their sadaqat al-fitr. These are issues in which the grandfather differs from the father in the apparent narration, and he does not differ in this narration of al-Hasan. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 1/399] 240 Sadaqat al-Fitr The Book of Zakat And for his slaves for service, his mudabbar, and his umm walad, even if they are disbelievers; not for his mukatib, nor for his adult child, nor his wife, nor a jointly owned slave, nor a runaway slave except after his return; likewise the usurped and the captive. And it is half a sa' of wheat, or its flour, or its groats; or a sa' of dates, raisins, or barley; which is eight ratls by Iraqi measure. And it is permissible to give value, and it is better when one finds what he needs because it is quicker to fulfill the poor person's need. If it is a time of hardship, then wheat, barley, and what is eaten are better than dirhams. The time of obligation is at the dawn of the day of Fitr. So whoever dies or becomes poor before it, or becomes Muslim, or becomes rich, or is born after it, it is not obligatory upon him. It is recommended to give it before going out to the prayer ground. It is valid if one gives it early or delayed, but delaying is disliked. Each person gives his fitr to one poor person. There is disagreement on the permissibility of dividing one fitr among more than one poor person. It is permissible to give what is upon a group to one person according to the correct view. And Allah is the granter of success. And for his slaves for service: He generalizes it so it includes the indebted, hired, and mortgaged slave if he has the means to pay the debt; the slave who commits an offense intentionally or accidentally; the slave vowed to be given in charity; the slave whose emancipation is suspended upon the arrival of the day of Fitr; and the slave whose corpus is bequeathed to one person and his service to another; then it (the fitr) is upon the one who is bequeathed the corpus, unlike maintenance which is upon the one who is bequeathed the service. And he indicated by his saying 'for service' that he does not give fitr for his runaway slave, nor for the usurped slave that is denied, except after his return, and then it is incumbent for the past; nor for his captive slave because it is out of his hand and control, so he resembles the mukatib; nor for his servant by hire or loan. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq with modification: 1/400] And his mudabbar: mudabbar is a slave to whom his master said: 'You are free after my death,' for example. And his umm walad: umm walad is a slave woman who bears a child from her master, and the master claims the child's lineage. Even if they are disbelievers: meaning even if these are disbelievers. And a jointly owned slave: meaning he does not give fitr for a slave between two or more partners. 'What he needs': meaning from these types from which fitr is given, if the time is a time of hardship. 'And it is valid if given early': He indicated by its generality that there is no distinction between one period and another as in al-Hidayah, and this is correct. According to Khalaf ibn Ayyub, it is permissible to give it early after the beginning of Ramadan, not before. It is said it is permissible in the last half of Ramadan. It is said in the last ten days. According to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, it is not permissible to give it early at all, like udhiyah, and it is invalidated by the passing of the day of Fitr because it is an act of worship specific to the day of Eid, so it is invalidated by its passing, like udhiyah is invalidated by the passing of the days of Nahr. We say: It is a financial act of worship that is not invalidated after obligation except by payment, like zakat; and udhiyah is not invalidated but the obligation transfers to giving in charity with its value; this is because the act of worship in shedding blood is not rationally understood, but is known by law in specific days, while the aspect of worship in giving charity is rational, supplying the need of the needy, so the time of performance is not restricted to a specific time. [Al-Kifayah with addition: 2/232] And it is
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr from Nur al-Idah, a matn in Hanafi fiqh. It presents the rulings of sadaqat al-fitr in a concise manner suitable for memorization. The rulings are stated without proofs, though the accompanying commentary provides some rational justifications. The passage covers the conditions of obligation: free Muslim, owner of nisab (beyond basic needs), time at dawn of Eid al-Fitr. It defines nisab as sufficiency, listing basic needs. It then discusses on whose behalf one pays: self, young poor children, and the dispute regarding grandchildren. The grandfather issue is detailed: in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), the grandfather does not pay for grandchildren, but there is a preferred narration from Imam Muhammad that he does. Next, the passage lists for whom one pays from his slaves: slaves for service, mudabbar, umm walad (even if non-Muslim), but not for mukatib, adult child, wife, jointly owned slave, runaway, usurped, or captive. The amount is half a sa' of wheat or its products, or one sa' of dates, raisins, or barley (8 Iraqi ratls). Paying the value is permissible and preferred except in times of hardship. The time of obligation is dawn of Eid; those who die, become poor, embrace Islam, become rich, or are born after that are not obligated. It is recommended to pay before going to the prayer ground; early payment is valid, delay is disliked. Distribution: one person's fitr to one poor person is the norm; dividing one fitr among multiple poor is disputed; paying multiple fitrs to one person is permissible. The commentary elaborates on each point, including definitions and exceptions.

Key Terms
صاع (sa'): A measure of volume; here 8 Iraqi ratls (approx 3.2 kg for wheat? For barley, similar).
قيمة (qimah): Cash value or equivalent monetary amount of the required food item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, when is early payment of sadaqat al-fitr permissible?
A) Only after the middle of Ramadan
B) Only during the last ten days of Ramadan
C) At any time before Eid without restriction
D) It is not permissible at all

Key Takeaway
Sadaqat al-fitr is half a sa' of wheat or one sa' of other grains; cash value is normally better; obligation attaches at dawn of Eid; each person's fitr should ideally go to one poor person, but combining is allowed.

Core Rulings of Sadaqat al-Fitr: Beneficiaries, Amount, and Timing
Rulings on who pays, amount, and timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of people for whom Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib and state the standard amount and timing.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib on the head of household for his dependents, including slaves and non-Muslim relatives.

This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah lays out the foundational rules of Sadaqat al-Fitr. Understanding who must be covered and what must be given is essential for fulfilling this obligation correctly. We will dissect the text's rulings on beneficiaries, the measure, and the time of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify obligatory beneficiaries
The head of household must pay fitr for his slaves (for service, mudabbar, umm walad) even if non-Muslim, but not for mukatab or adult children.
Includes slaves for service, hired, mortgaged, etc., but not runaway or captive.
2
Determine the standard amount
Half a sa' of wheat or its flour/groats, or a sa' of dates, raisins, barley (8 Iraqi ratls). Paying the value in money is permissible.
Value is better in normal times; grain is better in hardship.
3
Note the time of obligation
Obligation begins at dawn of Eid al-Fitr. Those who die or become poor before, or are born after, are exempt. Recommended before Eid prayer.
Early payment is valid (covered in next lesson).
4
Recognize key exceptions for slaves
No fitr for runaway, usurped, or captive slaves until they return or are recovered.
5
Understand the permissibility of giving fitr to one person or group
Each person's fitr can be given to one poor person, and it is permissible to give the fitr of a group to one person.
